“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
** The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Ib the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Rank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System-one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by 'the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the cl a’ms of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
tothebushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongiest -proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another,
i The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations Ihe cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
I straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
! public.

The spirit of the times is mostly illicit.
Allison, of Canonbar, will this year shear
the largest number of sheep in N.S. Wales —
210,000; Boorara and Gurley come next with
140,000 each.
Editor Bulletin, —You say : “ Glorious
Waterloo was fought on the blessed Sawbath.”
So were Dunbar and Worcester, when the Coven-
anters got specially glorious drubbings.-—J.B.
Dear Bulletin, —Your advice that jurie B
dole out their own mercy and trust neither Judg e
nor Executive, hits the nail on the head. That ha s
been our view up here for some Six year B
ago I served on a N.Q. jury which tried a young
clerk for embezzling -£3. The charge was proved
to the hilt. The Judge was brief; the jury’s
course was clear. They didn’t take it. They
acquitted the accused, whose conduct ever since
has been at least as edifying as if he had been
branded a gaol-bird or had spent 12 months on a
plank-bed.— Jim Crow, C.Q.
In an address recently made before the
Chambre Syndicate des Produits Chimiques in
Paris, Mr. Berthelot proposes to utilize the central
heat of the earth by sinking a shaft or shafts to a
depth of 4000 metres. At the bottom of these
shafts he believes that a temperature of 160° to
200° Cent, would be found, and the pressure
developed could be used for running machinery.
M. Berthelot believes also thafat this depth we
would reach a source of thermo-electric energy
almost without limit.
Wilhelm 11., says an English paper, is not
so tender-hearted to criminals as his august grand-
father, who had an attack of neuralgia and a
series of bad nights as soon as he had signed a
death-warrant. During his reign of 27 years as
King of Prussia and German Empetor, only 2r3
executions took place, and no woman was sent to
the block, the guillotine not being used in Ger-
many. But, since William the Strong’s accession
to the throne, 126 criminals have already suffered
death, out of whom eight were women. The
present executioner is a gentleman called Herr
Reindel, and, as in the case of a favourite actor,
as he performed last week his hundredth execution,
numerous telegrams of congratulation were sent
him from various parts of the kingdom to
Gleinitz, where the interesting solemnity was to
take place. An hour before the execution he
received as a token of felicitation and good wishes a
superb basket of Marechal Kiel roses; one of
them he put in his buttonhole, the others were
placed next to him on the scaffold.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
At Melbourne Democratic Club’s seventh
annual dinner, held on 4th July, 150 hungry
democrats sat down. Once a year this much-alive
institution lays itself out for a birthday spree, and
each year does' it better than the time before.
There was a free-and-easy dress-as-you-please air
about the affair, a solidity about the victuals, and
an absence of a lot of the usual silly, meaningless
toasts. Little Dr. Maloney was greeted with
“ Hello, Bill,” from all parts when he arrived, and
Hancock with “ What price, Jack?” while Tren-
with, who daily grows more like a well-con-
ditioned duck, waddled in amidst subdued
applause, which seemed to say that 1 at is a crime.
It came out that the club was started seven years
ago by five thirsty revolutionaires who wanted to
get a drink one Sunday, and either couldn t, or, if
they could, didn’t like the sneak-thief fashion in
which they were forced to do it. That quintette
thereupon formed the Democratic Club, whose
roll-call has multiplied up to the number of 550.
The club, it seems, has, according to President
Ellis, four distinct varieties of enemies. The
Sabbatarians snort at it because it holds Sunday-
morning boxing contests and Sunday-evening
concerts ; the teetotallers because it, like all other
clubs, sells liquor to members on all days and at
all hours; the licensed victuallers for the same
reason ; and all right-thinking persons because it
is a strong anti-fat influence, and makes possession
of an elector’s right a sine qua non of membership.
Some good songs, &c., were more or less mal-
treated during the evening; among them was
“The man who broke the bank,” See., sung by a
genius who had the temerity to don the horrified
Trenwith’s shiny belltopper and assume his silver-
mounted walking-stick for theatrical effect. Much
of the success was due to‘the fact that the gold-
spectacled and necktieless Jos. Symes at frequent
intervals during the proceedings interjected
“H’ah, h’ah.”

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added, where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tiie Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
tn the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
• Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or News paper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.
LONDON.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
xfter the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, K.C.
Also in tub Transvaal \~The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also: It. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queens-
land and Soutb Australia.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £l, with 3s. 3d.
added for postage.
No papers will be sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this rule.
Notice to Subscribers.
Coloured wrapper denotes expiration of subscription.

AUSTRALIA.
Owing to Governments of the above-named
provinces having imposed Half-penny postage on
ad papers from Ist Jidy, 1894, agents are in-
formed from that date the wholesale rate for
The Bulletin will be altered from Four Shillings
to Four Shillings and Sixpence per dozen.

SATURDAY, JULY 21, 1894.
A Strange Old Law.
The civil and criminal codes of Australia are
remarkable, not so much for what has been
deliberately put into them as for what the
local law-rnakers have forgotten to take out
of them. When Australia had its first
beginnings it took over, in one solid mass,
the statute law of England, and as much of
that law was obsolete, semi-obsolete or
practically forgotten this country did not
know, and doesn’t know to this day, what it
really did take over on that memorable occa-
sion. Old, dead, mouldy statutes which few
living men ever heard of lie about like loose
boards in the dark, and no one knows of
their existence till, once in a decade or so,
someone steps by accident on the end of the
hidden thing, and it rises up suddenly
and hits him in the eye amidst the ob-
scurity. Even on these occasions, however,
no Government makes any serious effort to
remove the obstruction. Governments,
as a rule, find great advantage in the present
hap hazard system. 'I he Cabinet, through
the medium of the Attorney-General and
the Crown Law Department, is virtually the
power which sets the law in motion, or allows
it to remain motionless, as the case may be.
There are existing, within its knowledge,
but without the knowledge of the general
public, scores of ancient, musty, barbarous
enactments which forbid, under heavy
penalties, hundreds of things of the illegality
of which the community has no semblance of
an idea. In 999 cases out of every thousand
the transgressors of these laws pass abso-
lutely unnoticed. But now and then some
person of obnoxious political or religious or
social views does what the other 999 have
done unnoticed, and then the law—of which
he is ignorant entirely through the premedi-
tated neglect of the authorities to enforce it
—is put in motion suddenly and with crush-
ing severity. He is the person who steps on
the loose board in the dark, and the law’s
administrators, who deliberately led him to
believe the board was sound, leave him to
take the consequences.
• • »
The latest victim of this man-trap and
spring-gun policy is J. A. Andrews, of
Sydxxey, who, being unable to pay a fine of
£2O, was sent, a few days ago, to gaol for
three months for selling an imprintless
pamphlet. Andrews is well-known as an
anarchist—about the most peaceful and in-
offensive anarchist who ever sought to con-
vert an aggressive world from the evil of its
ways. His pamphlet advocated anarchism
from an entirely peaceful standpoint ; it
was as devoid of all tendency towards blood-
shed and “ lawlessness ” as a tea-meeting or
a gathering of the Peace Society ; and it was
objectionable simply because nobody read
it, but everybody assumed, without read-
ing it, that it advocated dynamite and incen-
diarism, bombs, murder and sudden death.
Also, it actually did bear an imprint which
read as follows : “ Issued by J. A. Andrews,
late of Mudgee, and now of 491 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney.” As the object of the
imprint is simply to enable the authorities,
in the case of any publication which con-
travenes the law, to fix upon the person
responsible for its appearance, this fully
answered all requirements. It answered
them, in fact, much more fully than a tech-
nically legal imprint would have done, for
that merely gives the name of the printer, a
mechanical person who puts another man’s
offence into type without realising its full
import, while Andrews’ imprint took upon
himself the responsibility for the authorship
as well as all other matters. Nevertheless,
because he inadvertently neglected to give
the name and address of the artisan who set
the type, the dead and forgotten law was set
in motion with the above result.
• * •
If the said law, however tyrannical it may
be, was equally administered, there would
be little to complain of. But how unequally
it is administered can be guessed by a study
of its provisions. It requires, among other
things, that the printer of any paper or book
whatsoever, whether meant to be sold or
given away, shall put thereon the name of
the town or city wherein he resides, and the
name of the street, lane, court or place in
that town in which he has his dwelling place
or usual abode. This elaborate statement is
to appear on the front of every paper which
consists of one leaf only, and in the case of a
publication which contains more than one
leaf it must appear both on the front and
last leaves. There are a few unimportant
exceptions to the rule, such as bank notes,
bills of exchange, bonds, and the like, but
everything, excepting newspapers, which
can be included under the term literature in
its widest sense, is subject to its provisions.
As regards newspapers, even more elaborate
restrictions come into operation. The
number of documents which must be filled
up, of affidavits which must be written,
signed, sworn, attested and filed with
various officials, and the number of formali-
ties which must be complied with before one
copy of a paper can be lawfully issued is so
vast and elaborate that, in all reasonable pro-
bability, there are not, at the present time, a
dozen legally issued newspapers in New
South Wales. Also, there is, in all human
probability, not one book or pamphlet among
every ten that are published in the province,
which does not make the publisher, the
seller, and even the person who gives it
away to a friend, liable to a fine of £2O for
each individual copy.
* » *
These regulations apply to books and
papers published in the province. The same
law, however, covers —most likely without
any knowledge or intention on the part of
any one concerned—all imported papers in
its elaborate provisions, and does it in
so fearful and wonderful a fashion that,
as an able Sydney barrister long ago
informed The Bulletin, every separate in-
dividual who sells any British or other
foreign paper is, in all probability, liable to
an enormous penalty for every copy sold or
offered to the public. Also, in all likelihood,
the individual who gives away any such
paper is equally liable. These penalties may
be recovered by anyone who likes to take
proceedings, and it is only the general
ignoi’ance of the law, through its constant
non-enforcement, which prevents a whole-
sale and profitable trade being doxie in the
matter. The same statute makes every
person virtually a special constable for the
purposes of the Act, and authorises him to
seize and drag before a J.P. any individual
selling,- delivering, offering or exposing for
sale any paper or book which does not com-
ply with all the regulations made and pro-
vided —which practically means that any
person may seize and arrest any newsagent,
bookseller or publisher in the province.
• # *
This nefarious law is allowed to exist
simply because it is hardly ever enforced,
but while it does exist it enables the Govern-
ment to crush small and uninfluential poli-
tical opponents almost at its discretion. It
was enforced with brutal severity in the case
of the unfortunate Andrews and his harm-
less pamphlet, which pamphlet did bear an
imprint, though a technically insufficient
one ; it is not enforced in the case of the
National Ass, which, at the very same time,
was publicly circulating political leaflets with
no imprint whatever. Also it is nevei 1 , by
any chance, enforced in the case of any big,
influential paper, or any “right-thinking”
publication of any kind whatsoever. The
Government, in fact, takes it upon itself to
decide when the law must be obeyed and
when it may be broken with impunity, and
it always decides that it may be broken with
impunity by the rich, or influential, or
respectable, or obtrusively “ right-think-
ing ” person, and by no other. Under
such circumstances, the so-called “liberal
institutions ” of the country are a de-
lusion and a mockery. The Government
which can practically abrogate laws at its
own pleasure is as autocratic as the Govern-
ment which can make laws at its own plea-
sure. It is the business of the legal
authorities to enforce the law as it stands,
and in its entirety, until it is amended by
Parliament, and it has no more right to
condone the offence of the man who issues
National Ass manifestoes without an imprint
than it has to condone the offence of the
man who commits murder.
* • #
The surest and most speedy way to-
wards the abolition of the multitude
of tyrannous old statutes which Australia
inherited from its English forefathers is the
formation of a democratic association to
secure their constant enforcement. If such
an associatioxx were formed, with abundant
funds subscribed by the democracy, with the
best legal talent which could be procured,
and with an army of informers paid strictly
by results, it could, under capable manage-
ment, achieve wonders. Its business would
be to hunt up every old, wicked, forgotten
enactment on the statute-book and enforce
it to the bitter end against all classes, its
owxx included. If the brutal Sabbatarian
law were so executed against every shop-
keeper, every railway and tram official, every
rich man’s coachman and flunkey, everyone,
in short, who breaks the Day of Rest and
who can possibly be dragged into the
net, the whole business of the inferior courts
would be blocked by the incalculable mul-
titude of offenders, and a special sessioxx of
Parliament would be called in red-hot haste to
deal with the matter. If the law under which
Andrews was convicted were enforcedagainst
every printer and publisher in the land,
from the owner of the biggest daily paper
down to the dispenser of theatrical pro-
grammes at the door of a tenth-rate “gaff,”
its radical reform would be achieved within
a month. The same process would apply to
every other such enactment on the statute-
book. And, possibly, while digging among
the debris of the musty past, some forgotten
law might be discovered which prescribes an
appropriate punishment for those authorities
who are paid to enforce the law ixx its
entirety, yet only enforce it at their own
pleasure and convenience. Then the Crown
Law authorities who prosecuted Andrews
for a technically deficient imprint might be
prosecuted—and perhaps gaoled—in their
turn for their glaring and culpable failure to
prosecute the National Ass magnates on
similar grounds. Considering that the
National Ass has included all the members
of the New South Wales Government in its
published list of candidates, the neglect of
the authorities to proceed against it for its
recent breach of the law relating to publica-
tions looks especially significant.
Caesar’s Column.
When Ignatius Donnelly wrote the grue-
some tale of prophecy which he entitled
“ Caesar’s Column,” it is questionable if one
person in a hundred thousand took him
seriously, or regarded him as anything save
a grotesque romancer. His story of the
destruction of a whole world’s civilisation
—of a general upheaval of the Goths and
Huns throughout the entire globe, and their
frantic resolve to leave not one brick upon
another of all the works of man —seemed too
vast and purposeless ever to become even the
semblance of a reality. Yet the doings of
the last few weeks in the United States have
brought that semblance of a reality appre-
ciably closer, and the tale of Caesar’s Column
has come near to being enacted on a micro-
scopic scale at Chicago and various other
cities. It failed, partly because the Caesar
himself was wanting, and without him the
uprising was merely a frenzy of disconnected
atoms with no leader and no central organis-
ation worthy of the name ; and partly
because the time, like the Caesar, had
not arrived. The incubus of capitalism has
grown so heavy that 200,000 men or more
were ready and willing to declare a war of
annihilation against the dominant caste, and
to raze to the ground everything which syrm
bolised its hated existence. If the present
order of things endures, it is only a matter
of time and arithmetical progression until
20,000,000 men are ready to take up the
same cause. Then, providing the Uesar is
forthcoming, the prophecy of Ignatius
Donnelly will be fulfilled. The rioters who
held Chicago last week only wanted
as their leader one iconoclast of com-
manding intellect, and they would pro-
bably have laid the pork city in ashes.
If the same causes which called them
into existence continue to operate, the next
century may see an uprising which will
desolate the land from Panama to the St.
Lawrence, and give every combustible thing
in the United States to the flames.
* • #
The American Union is now, without ex-
ception, the richest State upon this earth.
Also, it has more millionaires than any other
nation in all the five continents, and it has
brought the doctrine of private enterprise to
a degree of perfection unknown in any other
civilised country. By reason of its policy of
private enterprise it is par excellence the land
of rail way-wreckers, stock-jobbers, fraudu-
lent syndicates, bowelless corporations, and
financial robbers of every kind and degree.
It has at one end of the scale the richest pluto-
cracy on earth; at the other a vast army of
tramps, paupers, anarchists and all manner of
frozen-out waifsand strays of humanity. When
it has ceased to be a “ new country ” —when
there is no longer a West as an escape-valve
for the crowded East, and the vacant lands
are all occupied, and the curse of private
enterprise has spread all over the union—
then it wiH have more tramps and more
anarchists than any other country on the
face of this sorrowful globe. The same
causes which have multiplied millionaires
and paupers in the past will, unless they are
remedied, multiply billionaires and paupers
in the future. And, meanwhile, the great
middle class which fills the gap between
these two extremes is being eaten up by
competition and private enterprise, And will
continue to be eaten up, while the present
system lasts, with increasing velocity. And,
as it declines, the time steadily approaches
when the Astors and the Vanderbilts will
be hanged by the coming Caesar in Madison
Square, and a war of extermination will be
declared against the whole scheme of
society.
# * #
The situation’ of the United States is a
strange and wonderful anomaly. There is
more wealth to be plundered there than, in
any other country. There is more corrup-
tion and more robbery by the processes of
private enterprise than in any other allegedly
civilised land. The aristocracy of wealth,
whose very existence is a perpetual challenge
to the ever-increasing masses of the poor, is
wealthier and more arrogant and more
garish in its brutal magnificence than any-
where else between the two poles. There is
more education, to teach the masses what
they are and what they might be, than in
the great majority of countries. The burden
of capitalism weighs more heavily on the
people than it does even in Europe And yet
the only reliable foi'ce which stands between
the plutocracy and utter destruction is some-
thing less than 30,000 indifferently -dis-
ciplined Federal troops. The militia of the
various States has been called out over and
over again to quell labour disturbances, and
has almost invariably refused to take action
in defence of “ law and order.” The habit
of military obedience was just sufficiently
strong in this citizen soldiery to prevent it
openly joining the insurrectionists, but, as a
rule, it has stood aloof and regarded the
insurrection with cheerful indifference. The
handful of Federal soldiers is actually the
only force which compels 60,000,000 people
to submit to the exactions of the most bowel-
less, the most aggressive, and probably the
most ruinous financial oligarchy upon eanh.
The whole system is an inverted pyramid
balanced upon its apex ; yet, though it is
always tottering, somehow or other its
fall is long postponed. In Europe, far less
burdensome tyrannies are propped up by
500,000 bayonets, and exist only because
some genius of bygone days discovered
that man is so subservient to mere habit that
he only needs to be drilled for a while,
according to a certain cast-iron formula, and
dressed in a blue or grey or red uniform, and
he will fight to the bitter end on behalf of
his slave-driver against his own class. But
in America the governing class has discovered
that men can be kept in slavery to syndicates
and corporations by the mere habit of
believing that they are free, and, therefore,
the Astors and the Vanderbilts have not
begun to dangle in Madison square—not as
yet.
# # •
Still, granted a continuance of the present
conditions, and the greatest and most deso-
lating Labour war in history will have to be
fought out in the United States sooner or
later. For the masses there is no visible
means of redress save by force. The
States have practically manhood suffrage;
but the machinery of the two great political
parties is so complete and far-reaching chat
the country has merely the barren privilege
of deciding whether Democrats or Repub-
licans shall hold office. The “bosses” of
these two factions maintain a feeble pretence
of differing about certain small points con-
nected with the tariff, so as to give to the
nation the erroneous impression that it has
a voice in deciding how it shall be governed,
but, in reality, the popular decision decides
nothing of any moment. The parties are
equally bought and owned by rings and
trusts and corporations, and the Govern-
ment, like the country, is run by private
enterprise. It will probably take twenty
years of organisation to create a dominant
People’s Party, as opposed to the two par-
ties of capital, and when it is created its
leaders will be bought by the almighty dollar
like the rest. Nowhere in all the world
is private enterprise so supreme as in
the States; it owns everything, even
the national suffrage ; it runs prac-
tically everything, from the Government
and the courts of justice downwards ; and it
is everywhere. The Labour movement in
America is practically a war against this
joint-stock incubus, and that, to all intents
and purposes, means that it is a war against
the whole local civilisation. To abolish the
present system of capitalism by violence is


to leave hardly one brick standing on another
throughout the Union. And to abolish it
otherwise than by violence seems just now a
very far-off possibility indeed.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Painful Business.
In Melbourne Banco Court, the other day, the
legatees under the will of Charles Ibbotson,
of Geelong, applied for an order to compel their
trustees to make good a deficiency of £5400 in
the estate. Said trustees were a pair of soli-
citors (formerly in partnership) and an un-
fortunate surgeon, who had been pressed into
the job by the heirs of Ibbotson, for the pur-
pose, no doubt, of keeping an eye upon the two
lawyers. The blame for the £5400 deficiency
appears to rest with one only of these profes-
sional caretakers. More than half of the
money was paid into their partnership account
by the defaulting worthy, “and has not been ac-
counted for. ” In plain English, one of the lawyers
was alleged to have lost or pocketed all the
missing money, and, as he made no defence
in the case, his profound silence is ominous.
Nevertheless, he is, or was lately, seen about
Melbourne, looking quite easy in his legal mind.
If a trustee, who isn’t a lawyer, pays other
people’s cash into his account and fails to pro-
duce it when called upon, he runs an excellent
risk of going to gaol. The difference between
a shortage in the cash of a trustee and a
shortage in the cash of a bank-teller is im-
perceptible in such a case, A lawyer, however, has
seemingly a license from the law to be short in his
cash with impunity. Judge Holroyd referred
to the delinquency in question as a “painful
business,” chiefly painful, possibly, because it
attracted further public notice to the disre-
putable condition of the legal brotherhood.
The word “fraud,” or a mention of criminal
proceedings, was never spoken in Court,
or if spoken, was certainly not reported
by the press. No “great” newspaper ex-
posed the full dimensions of this disgraceful
scandal, and nothing appeared to come of the
action beyond an order that accounts should be
taken. “ Further directions and the question
of costs would be reserved.” The Judge
couldn’t even make up his mind right away
that the trustees should pay costs, though he
was prepared to “relieve” two of them (in-
cluding the lawyer who didn’t account for the
missing cash) for continuing their duties to the
estate. As regards the unknown “ other direc-
tions,” it is probable that they will not include
a direction to seriously prosecute the cronk
attorney.
The Conspiracy Farce.
A few days since a Melbourne importer, one
J. K. Megredy, was acquitted on a charge of
conspiracy to defraud a French manufacturing
firm of a large sum of money, the proceeds of
“account sales.” The French firm’s goods had
been sold, and the cash for them was not forth-
coming. Practically, it was, prima facie, a
case of embezzlement of goods. Megredy, not
being the whole of the Melbourne agency house
himself, was charged with conspiracy with his
partners to rob their foreign principals, but the
said partners were not present. The Crown
prosecuted Megredy only, in the absence of the
other gentlemen, and the Crown counsel having
clearly shown that the French traders were
mulct of £9OOO, told the jury that an acquittal
would indicate that fraud was a recognised
business custom in Melbourne. The significance
of the barrister’s peroration was intensified by
Judge A’Beckett’s comment upon it. His
Honor was glad to have an opportunity of
informing the jury that “ a verdict of acquittal
in this case would not indicate any low tone of
commercial morality, or indifference to the
frauds which, according to the evidence for the
prosecution, were committed by Mr. Megredy.”
Mr. A’Beckett was not virtuously indignant;
he didn’t spurn the thought that Melbourne’s
financial morality was already open to grave
suspicion, he simply devoted himself to a legal
explanation of how Megredy might be dis-
charged without a stain upon the jury’s
character. In other words, the Judge under-
took the not very pleasant duty of showing that
another Crown prosecution was qualified to
end in a hair-raising fiasco. Hear Justice
A’Beckett addressing the jury :
According to the evidence of the prosecution, Mr.
Megredy committed certain frauds as to which you may
entertain the strongest feelings, and perhaps the strongest
desire to punish, but what Mr. Megredy is being tried for
in-this case is not for committing certain frauds himself,
for which he is personally amenable to justice, but he is
being tried for another offence, as to which, but for their
absence, others would stand in that dock and have to
plead as he pleaded to that charge. He is charged with
the offence of combining and conspiring with others to
do certain things, and, however bad those things are, if
you think that others did not combine with him to do
them, he escapes, not by reason of his own innocence, but
by reason of the fact that you are not convinced of the
guilt of others,
And much more to the same effect. The more
guilty Megredy, the more he must be let off,
explained the Judge, and the jury, after two
hours’ deliberation, found that they had no
proof of the guilt of others, therefore Macredy
was acquitted. The Crown authorities had
charged him with conspiracy, and so the case
failed, as other conspiracy cases have failed
before this. And it is difficult to avoid the idea
that Judge A’Beckett’s summing-up must have
seemed to the jury very like a legal apology for
the failure of conspiracy prosecutions in
general. The most unsatisfactory feature of this
strange business had yet to come, however.
Two or three days after Megredy’s acquittal
the Argus, for reasons that may be guessed at,
published the full text of the Judge’s summing-
up. In another column of the same issue was
printed a letter from Megredy commencing—
“ When you publish on Monday the whole of
Mr. Justice A’Beckett’s charge to the jury.”
Megredy somehow knew that the Argus
intended to direct public attention to the pecu-
liarities of this case, and was ready with a
letter in defence of what he knew would be
roughly estimated as a miscarriage of justice.
The Bulletin strongly recommends Mr.
Megredy, who has been acquitted on a techni-
cality in the conspiracy charge, to demand an
individual trial, in order that his character may
be decisively cleared.
The Pope or Lemonade?
Editor Bulletin. —Pascal and others have
complimented the R.C. Church upon its faculty
of being all things to all men—and women. It
leads, not drives, its votaries. While giving
due prominence to Hell, it does not make a joy-
less life the condition of Heaven. It has allow-
ances for feeble Human nature. Protestantism
insists upon the whole hog; Catholicism is
satisfied with a little bit to begin with. It
humours the individual, suits its doctrine to
circumstances, and makes the punishment
fit, not the crime, but the criminal.
Hence the prosperity which the Protestant
sects are never tired of envying. If a man
must have a creed, self-interest should surely
make him a Catholic, since then he can have a
good time in both worlds ; while to be a good
Protestant it is necessary to mortify the flesh,
and accept “ skim-milk here for the sake of
butter beyond the clouds.” It is Calvin and
Knox, not Escobar and Liguori, who would
rob us of our pleasant little vices.
How often do you hear a priest raving
for prohibition ? But, all the same, a
gentle suasive influence is continually being
exercised on the drunkard ; and not Catholic,
but Protestant countries, are the most drunken.
In which Austialasian province is the success
[i.e., the failure) of prohibition most probable ?
In Maoriland. In which province is Catholic-
ism least powerful ? In Maoriland. In which
province is prohibition least probable ? In
Queensland. In which is Catholicism most
powerful ? In Queensland. Here are some
1891 census figures of population :
Maoriland, 13"9 per cent. Catholics—most prohibitionist*
S. Australia, 14‘7 „ ~ —next most.
Tasmania, 17'6 „ ~
Victoria, 21 - 8 „ „
Westraha, 25'3 „ I next least.
N.S. Wales, 25'5 „ ~ (
Queensland, 53‘6 ~ ~ —least prohibitionist.
The classification is a matter of common know-
ledge, so far as made, and probably the grada-
tion of fanaticism could be extended through
Tasmania and Victoria. Anyhow, the tendency
is obvious. Prohibition is driving Australia
into the arms of Rome. In proportion as
human nature revolts against the repression of
the natural man, so will Catholicism triumph.
When Australians are called upon to choose be-
tween the Pope and lemonade, they will shout
with one voice for the Pope.—Yours, B.
———♦
The Odious Afghan.
Dear Bulletin, —You quote Major Leonard
to the effect that “ the Afghan is the dirtiest
brute on record.” Is he dirtier than other
Eastern races ? He is certainly braver than
most. History has praised his mettle ever
since he fought with Darius at Arbela ; and,
though Alexander conquered him, the English
could not. He has defied half-a-dozen Rourke
larrikins to come on all at once—and in vain.
There is a common white-skin prejudice against
“ the dirty blacks ” ; but it is astonishing what
a number of whites contrive to get along with-
out a bath from New Year to Christmas. Lots
of women have only bathed on their wedding-
day ; lots of men are never wet all over at once
till they are laid out for the grave. And,
as a Bulletin correspondent pointed out
recently, “ it’s no use a man being white
unless he’s white inside.” The cry of
“Australia for the white man!” has made
a lot of mean whites look upon themselves
as the salt of the earth. When the Afghans at
Bourka had a festival lately, they killed a
number of sheep and bullocks, and distributed
meat, tea, sugar and flour among these gentry.
It would have surprised you to see how many
proud mean whites rolled up to take the black
man’s bounty. A number of white families are
living on the Afghans now, and taking the
scraps from their camps. Every Sunday night
some of the womenkind of Bourke (high and
low) come to see the Afghans dancing and
enjoying themselves. A carrier’s wife here has
been employed for some time past in making
calico suits for them at 3s, per suit, they find-
ing materials. The other day a local publican’s
wife offered to do them for 2s. The mean
Afghans would not accept the offer. I don’t
like the Afghan; he cannot mix with us ; in
some things he is a bit too good for us ; and I
think he is better out of the country ; but he is
more honest and manly than many of those
who jeer at him. —Yours, Billabong,
Gunny Khan, an Afghan camel-owner, pub-
lishes a remarkable letter in the Central
Australian (Bourke). Some extracts :
When camels were introduced, a great outcry was made
by a certain section of carriers, who said that, if allowed
to come in any great number, the camels would run the
horse and bullock teams off the roads. The Carriers’
Union was then in existence, by-laws and s hedule of
rates being fixed, and I made application to be allowed to
join the union. 1 offered, if they allowed me to join, to
bind myself not to introduce any more camels, and, fur-
ther, to inform other Afghans that it would be no use
their bringing camels to Bourke, as they would not find
sufficient "work for them. Even this promise would
not satisfy, and I warned them that I would
use all efforts to introduce more camels into the
district, and, it possible, smash their union. I then
induced Abdul Wadk to join me, and shortly afterwards
he and M. Green, who afterwards became my partner,
brought over some 120 camels from South Australia ;
eventually, Abdul Wade w r ent to India and brought over
some 350 camels, and to-day I have the satisfaction of
knowing the Carriers’ Union is no more, nor, as far as I
can glean, is there any possibility of its being resuscitated.
. . . It may seem somewhat ambiguous to say that I
have felt sorry for some of the carriers leaving, but they
had no one to blame but their union. I have always tried
to act justly to all parties, squatters, carriers and others,
and it they only acted justly to me, they would have
found I had not such a bad heart—in fact, I have always
opened my purse-strings for any charity or other good
cause for the benefit of Bourke. I have, of late, kept my
camels here simply out of spite: there are plenty of
other places in Australia where I can get as good a living
as here, if not a far better one ; and that is solely why I
am leaving. The Camel is a long-legged animal and can
cover an enormous distance within a short space of time.
If there comes any difficulty here and the squatters do
not support the camel-owners there might he a probability
of their clearing out and leaving them to the mercy of
the carriers.
This gives scope for hard thinking. Evidently
an Afghan’s head has brains in it.
J. M. Davies, brother of St. Matthew of that
ilk, lately furnished the Vic. Council with a
piece of his mind concerning the reckless ex-
penditure of public sympathy upon widows.
Previous speakers had inclined to think that
widows needed protection in financial matters
—a certain amount of shielding from trustees
and so forth, but Matthew’s brother denounced
these ideas as maudlin sentiment. Widows are
evidently a sore point with the busted-bank
crowd and their respectable admirers. They
wish to hear no more concerning the lone, lorn
female who was advised to place her dividends
to her credit instead of taking them out, and
who now goes around wondering why she can’t
get any money at all. She is a vastly over-
rated sufferer, it appears, and references
to her general bad luck ought to cease.
Indeed, references to all such foolish outsiders
as have lost money, and seem likely to start
losing money again when “ confidence” returns,
are absurd. They are likewise very wounding
to the feelings of financiers, who regret, “ no
one more deeply” (judicial gag) that they have
brought grief to —er —themselves. Under these
circumstances The Bulletin calls for silence
on the topic of the widow and the fatherless,
and the retired tradesman, who commits suicide
in commemoration of his departed savings.
Cheerfulness and contentment, and determina-
tion to look at the bright side of things that are
black all round, these are the proper things to
talk about. For the rest, silence. Hssh !

against Affleck Robertson, the philanthro-

unless he happens to be a member of either

must also admit a grave doubt concerning one

self and wife, and only second-class tickets for

Wynne’s well-meaning bill to amend the Com-
panies Act has reached the Victorian Assembly
after being considerably emasculated by
“ boom ” sympathisers in the Council, where it
first saw the light. One of these Council
alterations is shockingly humorous. Wynne
proposed to make auditors certify their opinion
as to a balance-sheet being “ so drawn up as to
exhibit a true and correct view of the com-
pany’s affairs.” The Council passed this clause
with a few additional words—“ as shown in
the books of the eompany.” Any lie written
in the precious books is to be read as truth by the
auditors, and duly authenticated. And howabout
the directors who maintain a false appearance of
ignorance by not looking in the books, where
the lies that they know to be lies are recorded ?
The Mercantile Bank books, for instance,
assumed the solvency of Daviesian companies
by taking their worthless shares in among the
good debts, and Davies was too busy to look
at the little mistake. In that case the auditors
saw that all was right “by the books,” whilst
the chairman wouldn’t, or didn’t, see that all
was wrong by the same volumes. No amended
Act will be worth much if it doesn’t make
directors responsible for such knowledge of a
company’s affairs as they must happen to
possess whether they look in the books or not.
The symptoms arising from the action of irritant
poisons, among which are arsenic and antimony,
are almost identical with those proceeding from
cholera, diarrhoea, colic, gastritis, enteritis,
ulceration, and other disorders of the intestines.
Similarly the characteristics of poisoning by
narcotics and neurotics, which include opium,
prussic acid, chloroform, and strychnine, are
difficult to distinguish from those of apoplexy,
epilepsy, tetanus, convulsions, and other cere-
bral diseases. Also, it is possible for a person
to die from the mere shock of a poison’s sudden
action, and, without evincing any symptoms of
poisoning, present all the appearance of having
succumbed to syncope proceeding from valvular
or other disease of the heart. When it is a fact
that in Victoria, for example, about 2000
deaths per annum are ascribed to the diseases
enumerated ; when further it is considered how
many in a million have motives for desiring the
death of certain of their fellows; and, finally,
when it is reflected that the medical profession,
like every other class, has its share of born
fools, whom no amount of education or experi-
ence can ever make anything else, some idea
can be got of the probability now and then
entertained that many deaths, put down to
innocent causes, may really be the consequences
of poisons criminally administered.
Another attempt to legalise the totalisator in
Victoria has failed. Ikey Mo is safe for another
year at least, and some of his Parliamentary
allies have probably swilled champagne at his
expense. As usual, the totalisator’s opponents
were sublimely indifferent to harmony in their
vocal efforts, although they kept well together
when it came to a vote. Frank Madden, re-
presenting the gambling aristocracy of horse-
owners, objected to the machine, because it
would, so he said, prevent the public from los-
ing sufficient money to enable owners to win
enough, whereby these national benefactors
would be forced to sell their studs. Then the
breed of horses would deteriorate, and the
Indian trade would collapse; also “hunting, and
the other joys of the sport,” would perish along
with that essential personage, Ikey. “Let
truth and logic, wit and reason, go, but
leave us still our good old Ikey Mo,”
said Madden in effect. The present system
of betting, according to that enlightened
lawyer, is tantamount to an enormous public
subsidy of the horse-export trade, whilst the
bookmaker is properly privileged to sneak most
of the aforesaid subsidy for himself. After
Madden had railed against the suppression of
horseracing and gambling by a Tote Bill, a
number of other members objected to the awful
increase of gambling that must, so they said*
result from the same measure. They were
afraid to encourage the things that Madden
was fearful of discouraging, and so they
followed at his tail into the lobby. The bulk
of the Opposition, however, wisely refrained
from saying anything at all. They voted.
Isaacs was the only Hebrew champion of Ikey
who ventured to speak, and he perpetrated the
ancient gag that a respectable law ought not
to legalise gambling, although he is well aware
that the law of Victoria treats welshing as a
crime. Apart from Isaacs, there were two
Harrises, two Salmons, a Lazarus, a Zox,
and a Bosisto among the majority. Not one
Jewish-looking name figured among the list of
Tote supporters.

Parses urges that payment of members should
be placed on the same footing in Australia as
payment to Privy Councillors in England
(Hingland, Hingland, Hingland !) — i.e. , that
any member who fell into poverty might apply
for a grant from the Crown. That is as foolish
or as monstrous a proposal as Australia has
been treated to during the whole discussion of
the subject. A Cabinet Minister in England
receives a fixed salary while in office, and on
losing office lie is allowed, if he pleads poverty,
to draw .£2OOO per annum while he lives.
Beaconsfield, Childers, and others have
done so. But, passing over the indefensible-
ness of even that in a democratic country,
how can it be supposed the principle
would act if applied to members of
Parliament in Australia ? First, as the
English pensioner cannot hold office and draw
the £2OOO at the same time, the Australian
member, to complete the analogy, should resign
or be thrown out before the subsidy would
become available. If, however, it be supposed
that a subsidised member retains his seat, the
question arises on what testimony has his
subsidy been granted. Manifestly the claim
must be approved by the Ministry of the day,
and just as manifestly that member becomes,
or will be subject to the public obloquy of
having become, a tool of that Ministry. Second,
a proposal to pay a member because he is poor
and not because he does work introduces into a
new sphere the cast-iron political cheek which
fortifies politicians in being kind at the tax-
payers’ expense to their own ilk.
Thus Sydney Evening News : “If labour is so
grossly in the wrong, and capital so clearly in
the right .... how comes it, then, that
capital has so rooted an objection to arbitra ■
tion ?” A bold and undaunted championship of
Australian labour, this? A fearless and just
denunciation of Australian monopolists? Net
much. The E. News isn’t built that way.
There are two little words omitted from the
above quotation just where the dots mark a
hiatus. And those words are “in America.”
Which quite alters the whole complexion of the
bombastic bravery of the tirade. Like its com-
rade-tories, the News always pretends to take
the part of the workers when the attacked
monopolists belong to somewhere on the other
side of nowhere ; but, whenever there is indus-
trial trouble under its very nose, it hasn’t
language at its command vehement enough
to express its horror and indignation at
the insolence, the presumption, and the auda-
city of the workers, and its grovelling rever-
ence for the monopolist with “ a stake in the
country ” and an advt. in the News. Moral :
Never trust the paper that shrieks at the
iniquity of the Russian knout and applauds the
usefulness of the Australian lash.
Ur. Matthews, of Albury (N.S.W.), recently
acquitted on a grave but unwarrantable charge,
has received from the Colonial Secretary’s office
an official letter, expressing regret, virtually
apologising, and offering on behalf of the
Government “ to reimburse any reasonable ex-
penses incurred in connection with the defence.”
The Bulletin does not object to this handsome
apology and compensation being tendered Dr.
Matthews, but it is tempted to ask whether
the New South Wales Government has seen fit
to send a similar letter and a like offer to the
bushman arrested in Queensland by a super-
intelligent New South Wales constable, and
dragged over to Moree (N.S.W.) as the sup-
posed murderer in the discovery known as “ the
Barwon mystery ? ” That man, concerning
even whose name the wires have been dumb,
has been discharged as a victim of mistaken
identity, but there has been no report of any
offer on the part of the Government to compen-
sate him. Voluntary Government compensa-
tion is for the man in broadcloth, not for the
man in moleskins.
Melbourne Chamber of Commerce has been
treated to a lecture on the local law of in-
solvency by P. D. Phillips, and if the C. of 0.
still holds its peace concerning that legal out-
rage the uncommercial community will draw
some bold conclusions. Of one solemn clause in
the Vic. Act the lecturer said : “It was remiss-
ness on the part of the Government that such a
scandal was not rectified,” though he omitted
to add that the “ remissness ” has been staring
each successive Government in its brazen coun-
tenance for years past. As for private com-
positions Mr. Phillips repeated the oft-told
fact that “ Victoria held to a system which
most civilised countries had long given up —a
system which enabled a certain number of
creditors to accept any composition that
they pleased, and to make it binding
and effectual for those creditors who did
not agree to it, utterly regardless of the
debtor himself. The Vic. system had been
used in a manner that was never contem-
plated (?), and men who were actually criminals
were let through—with some scandal, un-
doubtedly—but the Court was powerless to
stop them.” Mr. Phillips concluded with an
anecdote of one compositioner whose solicitor
had actually prepared a Id. in the £ arrange-
ment, when the insolvent, observing that only
friendly creditors were present, suggested that
a half-penny would meet the case. The lawyer
thereupon reduced the gorgeous offer by one-
half, and it was carried unanimously. But
nobody seems to expect an effort at reform from
the Chamber of Commerce. That august body
knows its own business too well, probably.

The Wild Cat Column.
The Victorian papers now declare in the plainest
language that the banks can’t go on paying 4jand 4J per
oent. on their reconstructed deposits, and that something
must be done to ease the strain. This probably means
more reconstruction. Interest has gone down consider-
ably since the famous arrangements were made, and the
bargain, which looked an exceedingly good one at the
time, now turns out to be very bad. If interest had gone
up, as was expected in some quarters, instead of going
down, the big pawnshops would doubtless have held on
to their bargain with the tenacity of the fierce, green
lobster; as it is, the case is different. In each instance
the banks retain power to pay off their liabilities at any
time, and those which are reasonably sound will probably
relieve the trouble by releasing the bulk of their deposits
and taking back those which offer at a lower rate, or else
they will secretly buy up a lot of their own depreciated
paper in the open market, as one institution has been
doing for some time back. But for those which dare not
release their deposits there seems no alternative, unless
things improve, save more reconstruction!

* * * *
One of the complications which led the Bank of
New Zealand into its present queer position is that
curious piece of financing by which it professed, some
years ago, to sell a lot of its land to the B.N.Z. Estates
Go. When the Maoriland boom exploded the bank found
itself with between three and four million pounds worth
of land which was not immediately saleable. This awful
lock-up of its capital had to be alleviated somehow, so
the Estates Co. was formed, and the land was transferred
to it. The humour of the situation lay in the fact that the
B.N.Z. held practically all the shares in the Estates Co., so
it virtually sold its own unsaleable land to itself. The only
cash in the transaction was about £1,335,000, which the
Estates Co. raised in London by selling 5 per cent,
debentures at a serious discount, said debentures to be
redeemed on maturity at a premium. All this practically
amounted to the fact that the bank borrowed on its
landed property, alleged to be worth considerably over
£3,000,000, less than half its value, at the alarming figure
of over 6 per cent. The Hebraic rate of interest goes to
prove that the lenders felt anything but confident about
the security. The interest on this awful transaction has
been a mill-stone upon the institution ever since. No
bank can afford to borrow at 6 per cent, in times like
these, and as the debentures don’t mature until 1910, the
prospects of relief are still very remote.
When the B.N.Z. becomes a State bank, which
It probably will do in a very few years, the position of its
two smaller local rivals, the Colonial and the National,
will be tolerably precarious. They have held their own
with difficulty against the big bank in the past, but
when it becomes a State-guaranteed institution it will
mop up all the good business and leave them only the
dregs. If the Government wants to do the fair thing, it
should take over the whole three and amalgamate them
into one big national concern. In 1891, shareholders in
the National B.N.Z. were giving as much as 50s. a share
to anyone who would take the investment off their
hands, and holders who couldn’t do this tried to establish
an alibi, or else fled into places of concealment. The
scare proved unnecessary, and N.B. shares have since
recovered somewhat, but the institution is not in a posi-
tion to compete with a State bank, and is not likely to be
for many years to come.
The new shares of the B.N.Z. {£7 10s. paid up)
have buyers now at 35s. Ten years ago the £10 shares
sold within a fraction of £28.
* * * *
Until the £2, 000,000 worth of State-guaranteed
preference shares are paid off ten years hence, no dividend
is to be paid on the ordinary shares of the B.N.Z., except
by consent of the Treasurer. This may work well in
Maoriland, but in one part of the world, which Tub
Bulletin knows of, it would simply mean that the
Treasurer wouldn’t consent unless ho was allowed an
overdraft.

The official receiver in London of the Mercantile
Finance, Trustees and Agency Co. (Melbourne), demands
“a searching investigation ” into the conduct of the Aus-
tralian directors. This was the famous institution which
somehow got through £600,000of paid-up capital, €320,000
of reserve fund, and something like a million of borrowed
money, and which seemingly doesn’t know to this day
how it was done. It is now being peacefully liquidated
by its own directors, who are thus invited to start a
searching inquiry into their own conduct forthwith.

Mat. Larkin’s brazen impudence hasn’t lost its
lustre in gaol, seemingly. His reappearance at South
Melbourne Police Court on three new charges was very
characteristic of dear old Mat. Dispensing with legal
assistance (though the Lord knows he could pay for
plenty of it), he got permission to sit at the barristers’
table, and there distinguished himself by insulting
accountant Simpson, who was testifying to the state of
the books. “ We don’t want your opinion, Mr. Simpson.
We know what it is worth,” snorted Matthias, and then
came the expected reference to Simpson’s little social
weakness. Financial criminals, convicted and otherwise,
are united in a great hatred for Simpson, the gifted un-
raveller of tangled figures and boss fraud exposer. He
has been clear-headed enough to discover swindles that
other people couldn’t detect as easily, and honest enough
to make them public in a way that was bound to excite
the wrath of Fat Man. It must have been Fat Man who
suggested Mat. Larkin's attack upon this valuable public
servant.
As far back as 1889, Mat. Larkin, in his private
capacity of estate agent, was sued for £3607 by a client
who couldn’t get a settlement of affairs from him. There-
upon Larkin, it is alleged, embezzled the money to meet
this claim from his unfortunate building society. There
doesn’t seem to have been the old excuse of “ mad land
boom speculation” in “honest Mat.’s” case. He wa’
probably (pending other people's cash long before lbs
boom eUrted.

The oableman sends out from Hingland the
truly important statement that some anonymous
person, calling himself “ A Depositor," has written to

to oable out to the poor, miserable Australian.

In London, advertising a new 00. with a
capital of £50,000 costs £lOOO, and printing, wrap-
ping, packing and postage of prospectuses costs £6
a thousand more. It is reckoned 50,000 prospectuses
should be sent out when raising £50,000 capital. There-
fore the prospectuses would cost £3OO. Add to this the
cost of advertising, and the promoter spends £l3OO for a
start. In Melb. you can float a Westralian Q.M. Co. of
40,000 shares at a cost of £so—for advertising and pro-
spectuses. For a capital of £lOO,OOO a London promoter
outlays £5OOO on ads, and printing.

Aarons’ Exchange Hotel 00. of Sydney is pro-
bably the most prosperous affair of its kind in Australia.
For the half-year it has declared a dividend of 8 per cent.
• * • ♦
About the only case on record where all its
opponents in the same trade don’t want another firm to
“ shut up shop ” arises in the South. One of them, whose
position is alleged to be hopeless, has such a tremendous
stock that if it is “sacrificed” at present, trade in that
line will be paralysed, so far as the survivors are con-
cerned. Hence the alleged cronk firm’s opponents desire
that it should keep afloat. The only question is, “Can
the bank interested afford to keep the concern going at a
loss ? ”

The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. of
Victoria, which has now been 16 years in existence, pre-
sents a cadaverous balance-sheet for the half-year ending
June 30. The profits for the 6 months amount to £2287
9s. Id., and a dividend at the rate of 7J per cent, per
annum absorbs £2250 of this, and leaves the co. just £37
9s. Id. to the good. This isn’t strengthening its position
to any marked extent. Out of the reserve-fund of £85,000
£65,000 is invested in freehold property, but as the receipts
for rent are only £632, and the expenditure for rent and
taxes is £795, the said investment seemingly yields a net
income of nothing at all, or thereabouts. Under these
circumstances, its value looks problematical. Even leav-
ing the £795 entirely out of the calculation, £632 isn’t
quite 2 per cent, per annum on £65,000, and a property
which only yields 2 per cent, on its nominal value seems
absurdly over-estimated for all practical purposes. There
is also a mortgage of *35,000 on the freehold, and the co.
has a similar sum invested to meet the liability when it
falls due, hut the interest earned on the £35,000 invested
is £320 short of the interest payable on the mortgage,
so the company’s property in reality only produces about
£3OO a year net—or less than 1 percent. All which seems
to suggest that it is a lamentably overrated investment,
and a millstoue round the co.’s neck. Apart from this
the institution has £79,000 invested (£lO,OOO in 4 per cent
Government stock, and the balance mostly on mortgage),
and the interest receipts average nearly 4J per cent.,
from which it would seem on the surface that the mort-
gages are mostly in a reasonably healthy state. The in-
come, however, shows a falling-off from last year,
and the co. spends £5830 in expenses for the purpose of
earning £5990 in commissions. Therefore, it only lives on
the interest on its own capital and reserve fund, and it
might as well shut up the trustee business altogether—
unless it is kept on for the sake of the management’s
health. This calculation doesn’t include interest on the
mortgage upon the office; when that is reckoned the
trustees business is a dead loss of about £l6O.

A Grenfell (N.S.W.) correspondent:—“There
are 19 proved gold-reefs on Grenfell, many of which are
being held on the “ nod.” A few are being squarely
worked. Capital would find good remuneration on any
of these reefs. The depths vary from 150 ft. to 400 ft.
The Homeward Bound, the prospecting claim of which
(six men’s ground) panned out £lO,OOO per man, is still
working. The reason most of the reefs have been lying
idle is that during the Gulgong rush Grenfell had very
wet weather, and as most of the miners had got through
the first gold-strata and could go no further on account of
the water, the majority cleared out for Gulgong. If a shaft
were sunk on any of the Grenfell reefs to the depth of
1000 ft., and driven for one or two hundred feet, it’s a
rail-road to a gooseberry that several reefs would be
out. 300 oz. of gold left Grenfell this last month of May,
the output of four reefs.—July 9.

At Wyalong, the new growl is that both War-
dens Slee and Sharp promised the field surveyors, who
have not come._ No one knows whether his neighbour is
mjning or residing as a trespasser. And, again, the
mining law has been broken by both Wardens, inasmuch
as instead of moving No. 1 up to the prospectors and so
on, spare ground has been left, and in more than one
instance the inter-miners have the best oi it. Survey fees
have been paid, and no surveys made.
* * ♦ ♦
The newagreement which has caused thestrikeak
the Sunlight (N.S.W.) mine seems to be a case of “ heads,
the owners win ; tails, the miners lose.” It is an attempt
to apply the sliding-scale to gold-raining. The men have
to keep up a minimum output of ore dressed in a par-
ticular fashion ; and their wage is to vary, not only with
the output, but also with the quantity of gold in the
stone ! That is to say, the miners take a large share of
risk without getting a corresponding share of profit. The
system is unfair, unless the miners become shareholders
in the 00. While they remain labourers only they should
be paid for the work they do irrespective of the value of
the stone. The price of gold is constant, unlike that of
coal. Supposing the average Sunlight yield were 20z.,
instead of 9dwts., would the directors ever dream of the
new agreement I
* • • *
An official report from Oarangula (N.S.W.)
states there are from 1100 to 1200 men on the ground,
with 50 or 60 payable claims. Allowing three men to a
claim,_ this means that nearly 1000 men are not making
anything. The acting-warden believes the field will be
permanent, and though many men are leaving many
remain.

• * * ♦
Yalgogrin (writes a Bulletin correspondent)
is 27 miles N.W. from Wyalong and 110 miles S.E. from
Hillston. Its population numbers about 400, and the
township was laid out by a progress committee, directed
by Warden Slee. It boasts 4 stores, 2 butchers, 2 bakers,
a workmen’s club, blacksmith’s shop, a sawmill, 2 J’s.P.,
and an Otis crushing plant, but has so far no police pro-
tection. The battery is now putting through 250 tons
stone from Thompson and party’s claim, which has a well
defined reef 3ft. to 4ft. 6in. wide (ironstone quartz),
carrying good gold. Alluvial is being found 7 miles from
here, on the Napier run. At present 12 payable claims
are raising stone, and some have 60 to 60 tons at grass,
awaiting their turn at the crusher. Warden Slee recom-
mends the resumption of Mrs. R. Payne’s conditional
purchase—a matter that should be attended to at once,
lor the area undoubtedly contains great mineral wealth,
and must lie untouched because the M. on P. Property
Bill practically prohibits mining on enclosed lands. The
resumption of the C.P. would open a field of labour for
hundreds of miners.
* • * •
One N.S.W. Minister is said, on what authority
The Bulletin knows not, to have, during the progress of
his electioneering campaign, wired to Labour-Bureau
chief Greer, warning the latter not to send any men along
to take the places of some strikers at a gold-mine within
the electorate for which he was a candidate. How do
the penal clauses of the latest Electoral Act interpret
this, if it really occurred.
• * ♦ •
Day Dawn, four miles from Cue, the present
capital of the Murchison, is struggling hard for the
supremacy, and may in a year be the bigger town. At
present, however, Cue has the start. At the recent land-
sale the blocks at Day Dawn, although but half the size
of the Cue blocks, brought twice as much money as was
realised by the Cue land. The pioneers who took up the
Day Dawn 12 or 18 months ago are, naturally enough,
disgusted at the action of the Government in allowing
capitalists to come in and buy the blocks over theirheads.
Having spent their money and time in opening out the
country, they consider that they should hare had a call
on the ground at upset price.

half-year’s product, 322,4520 z., topped Queensland’s by
26,0000 z.

• » ♦ «
Editor Bulletin, —When you come to Cool-
gardie don’t bring over a brace of horsepistols and a
cheap revolver. Arm yourself to the teeth with half-a-
dozen mouse-traps. Firearms are in small demand here,
but tucker and swags must be protected from the mice at
all hazards.
It is noticeable that the insane stampede of
hundreds of people coming over to Coolgardie without
any definite object in view, is quietly drawing to an end.
It seems to have struck them that gold cannot be picked
up in the streets, as they stroll about with their hands in
their pockets.
Things are now assuming a more healthy aspect.
Most of the reefs being opened up show well. The best of
them—mind you, I say “ the best”—even surpass the
average of good reefs, at similar depths, in places like
Charters Towers. This, I think, will prove to be the
“Towers"of W.A.
At a rough reckoning, I should estimate that
most of the alluvial now obtained here costs fully £2O an
ounce to win.
Trade is good, and the supply fully equal to the
demand. Horses, waggons, spring-carts, &0., are falling
in price ; also provisions and other necessaries, the hun-
dreds of teams now competing on the roads being the
cause. Business of most descriptions is no longer so
precarious and risky as in the past. For some time past
bricks have been made and brought into use upon the
field. And now the first house with a stone front is being
well and substantially erected in Bayley-street. The
three hanks here are busy night and day, and the Cool-
gardie Cyclist Express Co., floated almost solely here,
does good biz. A few months more will see Coolgardie no
longer a huge mining camp composed of bag-tents, tinned
meat and profanity, but a good solid place, with wide,
well-defined streets, good buildings (for W.A.), and the
natural business centre for all the outlying fields.
Fresh turnips, pumpkins, potatoes, onions,
oranges, &c., are now landed here and retailed in good
condition at a fairly reasonable rate. Business, generally,
is still hugely overdone ; the fittest only will survive; but
a great crowd are going to get a good living here. The
Qod-forsaken shanties and tents now passing muster for
post-offices, Warden’s Courts, hospitals, &c., are to be
shortly removed, and good buildings will soon take
the place thereof.—Yours, Harry W. Chbesbrodgh,
Bayley-st., Coolgardie, W.A.

Dear Bulletin, —You want the true “strength ”
of Coolgardie. Here you are : Coolgardie is at present a
“spieler’s," not a digger’s, field. There is lots of gold
there—brought there—mostly good Victorian, with heads
and tails. That Coolgardie will some day be a great reef-
ing field there is no possible doubt, but its future is
damned for years by the “ road-metal ” syndicates that
have been floated. There are numbers of claims there
that won’t go sdwts. to the 500 tons. If you have any
spare cash send it to me to get home with—you have far
more chance of getting it back. A pennyweight piece
found at Kurnalpi is a 10-ounce nugget at Coolgardie, and
a 1000-ounce nugget in the Argus, and 1000 more fools are
soon on their way to this “hell” with their earnings.
Coolgardie is bossed by rings and escaped Melbourne
land-boomers. The only people making money are store-
keepers, auctioneers, condenser-owners, grog-shoppers,
and camel-bosses. .Jumpers have done fairly well up to
date, and two ponies were offered to me to “ lep ” a
claim, but I was too much of a new-ohum then—l
wouldn’t refuse it now. There is absolutely no alluvial
left—Coolgardie has been dry-blown five times over.

The “Siege of Paris,” near One, has been
Jumped by one Lee. The claim is supposed to be one of
the good things on the Dead Finish. Some splendid stone
is seen in the paddock, and the mine was under offer
when Jumped for alleged neglect of labour-covenants.
The warden has warned people in several previous oases,
and decided in favour of the Jumpers.
• * • •
The Adelaide rich and clever division have gone
out to see the Londonderry, the prospectors whereof have
struck the reef at a depth of 50ft. in the crosscut from the
shaft. The reef was met a few feet from the shaft, and is
said to be as rich as it was a few feet from the surface.
Only the owners have seen the new development, and
they have gone on sinking.
* • • •
The report that the Londonderry was sold turns
out incorrect. Begelhole says he offered £50,000 for the
mine, including all specimens produced ; but the offer
was not taken. Oood quartz specimens, one worth £lOO,
have been found near the Londonderry, and fair alluvial
about four miles away.

The completion of the telegraph-line this week
will bring Coolgardie many days nearer the East.
* * * *
In a year or two, perhaps less, the trouble at
Coolgardie will be water, not want of water. The latest
is that a great influx has been met with at 155 ft.
in the Carnage mins. It spurts through the borehole and
work has been suspended owing to the rapid rise of water
in the shaft. Every shaft at Coolgardie that has yet been
sunk 120 ft. has got water. The Government bore, which
has been supplying the whole population, is only 130 ft.
The Brilliant, at the Twenty-Five Mile, struck watsr at
123 ft.

Alan Bright’s report draws attention to the
contrast between Towers calls for the half-year, £26,884,
divs., £127,151, and Gympie calls, £43,687, divs., £112,155;
and reasons that there is " an unaccountable lack of
■peculation on the Towers.” Thatisso. AlotofTowes
money is invested outside, while most of the Gympie
winnings go back into the mines. One lucky Gympie
speculator put £50,000 into Brisbane property in the
boom days, and the result has only confirmed the preva-
lent local opinion that the best way to lay out your divi-
dends is setting them to grow more dividends.

Mount Morgan half-yearly balance shows re-
ceipts, £489,000; expenses, £177,000 ; £300,000 being
paid in divs., and £15,000 in the div.-dutv which is
Queensland’s unique effort to tax wealth, and represents
a spasm of vengeance by politicians who had burnt
their fingers in the 00. The yield was 102,2260 z. from
65,076 tons, and the gold extracted is as high as 96 per
cent, of assay value. The problem now is the profitable
treatment of the immense quantity of low-grade ore, and
the manager is confident it can be done for 12s. per ton.

The yield from Herberton lode tin-mines is
decreasing, while Watsonville and Irvinebank just hold
their own. The stream-tin product is increasing slightly.
The total district tin-product for half-year ended June,
was 1030 tons.
♦ * • *
The bug-bear of N.S.W. timber-merchants just
now is the “ pocket timber-dealer.” This is a man whose
stock in trade consists exclusively of a pocket-book and
pencil, and he does his business right off his book, as it
were— i.e., he finds out that a building is to be started
somewhere; he goes to the architect, sees the plahs ahd
specifications, reckons how much timber will be needed,
and then gets the order, same to be delivered on the
ground, say, four months’ hence. When he has got
enough orders to make a cargo he (or a number of
him) cables to America and has his vessel out within
three months. Having to pay no rent, no interest, no
employes, he can do orders at 25 per cent, below the
ruling rate, and pay himself handsomely into the bargain.
Melb. Bi-metallic League has adopted as its
anthem the song of “Silver-threads among the Gold.”
• * • *
Captain Parfitt, of the P. and 0., passed an
examination the other day and procured a pilot’s certifi-
cate, thus saving his company about £5O in the Sydney
port on each visit. Sundry other ocean-going captains
have also got certificates, and a lot more intend getting
them, so local pilots will soon have plenty of time on
their hands.

Credit, says Professor Jevons, goes in cycles ot
10 years. There are first three years of depressed trade,
then three years of healthy trade, then two years of
excited trade, one year of bubble, and one year of burst.

Lady Hopetoun acknowledges receipt of
Aug. W. Juncker’s pretty song, “ I was Dream-
ing, ’ with which her ladyship is very pleased.
Miss Nellie Stewart has made a big hit with it in
Melbourne, where she has been singing it during
the past five weeks in “ Ma Mie Rosette,” at the
Princess’ Theatre ; it is the gem of the opera, and
always secures an encore. Mr. Samuell’s next
publication is a pretty “Slumber Song,” by Har-
rison White, dedicated to Miss Colbourne-Baber,
whose portrait appears on the cover. A special
edition is being printed for W. H. Paling and Co.,
Ltd., where advance orders may be booked. “ The
Duchess of York Waltz ” continues a firm favour-
ite. “I was Dreaming” is now on sale in Mel-
bourne by W. H. Glen and Co., Oollins-street, or
of the publisher, H. T. Samuell, Box 1077 G.P.0.,
Sydney.
