Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, J uly 17.
It is comforting to hear that at least one Mel-
bourne woman oan afford to buy a bit of good lace
and put some of her neighbours’ gown-trimmings
to the blush. A matron with an eye for the
elegant in Brussels point brought a gleam of glad-
ness into an old-established draper’s, the other
day, by actually purchasing—not merely looking
at—a piece of five yards at 20 guineas a yard.
I expect she was shown to her carriage with much
devotion after that. Such incidents set one think-
ing that drapery men ought to practise on bass
instruments and drums, for then a prize customer
could be played off the premises by a full band.
A semi-sacred air would meet the case nicely.
After all, our vice-regal Diana seems fated to
become the idol of the masses, even if she doesn t
supplant dear Lady Loch in the affections of the
“bong tong.” That narrative of her ladyships
catastrophe in the hunting-field, told by a privi-
leged eye-witness who carried a whisky-flask,
places the heroine in the centre of the stage with
limelight streaming down upon her blood-stained
beauty. Even the most matter-of-fact men, I
find, are evincing solicitude for the spirited lady’s
health. The subject of her nose quite agitates
them. Some of them would, I firmly believe,
mention the nose in their prayers, if they hadn’t
got out of the way of saying such things. For-
tunately, Dr. Fitz. promises that there shall be no
“permanent disfigurement,” which is satisfactory
in the future tense, but not suggestive of an early
reappearance in public. Saturday before the
accident Lady Hopetoun was looking her best at
Flemington races, in a well-off-the-ground blue
serge, and sable fixings. The short skirt gave the
“ Demmoline ” feet such a fine chance that the
Countess’s boots were as popular as Apsley’s
victory. She was, sportingly speaking, in rare
fettle that muddy day. The Manifold tip had
romped in, and the whole Guv’ment House crowd
had backed it.
The unco’ guid—why is it?—seem to be more
addicted to marrying and giving in marriage than
their livelier fellow-men. I hear that a pre-
eminently pious bank-director, at present on a
financial mission to London, has just announced
his engagement, or rather, re-engagement, on
blush-pink paper. This will be the good man’s
third matrimonial venture, and only the other
day he wept at his daughter’s wedding at Kew,
and painted, in harrowing terms, the desolation
of his widowed hearth.
There seems no lack of mean people, but the
champion unselfish man resides in the neighbour-
hood of Melbourne. He is a very well-known
suburban chemist, and was married recently.
Business is dull, and, after paying the clergyman
and other incidental expenses, funds for the
honeymoon were found to be strictly limited.
During the simple marriage-feast the bridegroom
carefully considered pros and cons. Realising
that a week’s change of air for two would cost pre-
cisely the same as a fortnight for one, he decided
that, from a hygienic point of view, the latter
would be more profitable. So he sent his wife to
a favourite resort, where she still is, no doubt
enjoying her honeymoon tremendously, while
Romeo stops at home to mind the shop and push
the pill trade.
At the Princess’ Theatre, “ Ma Mie Rosette ”
trips lightly towards the end of her sojourn, and
Browntow, quite at ease now, strains every nerve
to acquire his predecessor’s lady-killing knack.
Feminine critics in general aver that so far they
see no cause to blot out the memory of the “ dear
departed,” while the verdict of a fair society
oracle, re Brownlow, is “lovely on the stage, but,
in ordinary dress, only a large English lad ! ”
In some respects, of course, one can’t be too
“ Homey,” and of late every Melbourne marriage-
feast of any importance boasts a “real English
wedding-cake,” made to order in London. Its
sugared inscription, “ facsimile of Princess
May’s,” impresses the guests more than the
alleged cheques in sealed envelopes. It even
prevents the bride “ wishing she hadn’t,” when
hubby mangles his carefully-prepared speech, and
reveals himself in the unfamiliar (to her) aspect of
a drivelling idiot.
To return to “ Ma Mie Rosettethe gilt-edged
gardener’s daughter wears her hair in precisely
the same style throughout her varied experiences,
waking or dreaming. In real life, a brand-new
Marquise, dressed for a ball at a royal chateau,
would probably attempt an ambitious coiffure.
At all events, in the midnight scene, the
Stewart’s head-dress might be more in accordance
with the unwritten code of nocturnal fashion.
In Rosette’s time, a halo of meek braids would, I
believe, be no anachronism. The methods of ’94
are different, but few women, so far as I know,
court slumber in the chignons and Princess May
fringes of daylight.
Apropos, “curling-pins” are recommended as
an invaluable soporific. A friend (who, before
retiring to rest, occasionally flattens and combs
back her hair preparatory to reading a paper next
day on “the enfranchisement of woman,” or some
such topic) finds that sleep will not visit her eye-
lids until she gets up and pegs out her scalp in
orthodox fashion.
The Oamperdown estate, where Gov. Hopetoun
purposes spending the latter end of his Victorian
exile, and where the Countess will probably con-
valesce in this time of tribulation, is prettily
situated on the shores of a lake. The former
owner died some months back, and her daughter
married a few weeks later, so their home is now
available for vice-regal retirement. His Ex,
always has gushed over the beauties of Oamper-
down scenery, which pleased him all the better
from the luxurious conditions of life in those parts.
“Gov’ment House is shockin’ barracky, don’t
y’know !” The local squatters maintain the state
of feudal lords, and supply all their own domestic
needs, even to the making of gas.
It was at Oamperdown, as a press correspondent
remarks with a touch of reminiscent pathos, that
Gov. Hopetoun first practised polo; his tutors on
that occasion being the brothers Manifold, assisted
by scions of other illustrious houses.
Whose family-tree can hope to escape the
blighting influence of poor relations ? When
a party from Melb. Government House visited
Tasmania some years back they had a shocking
experience in point. Ever since then the merest
mention of the Tight Little Island disturbs vice-
regal equanimity, and in my Lady’s own apart-
ments it is the sure forerunner of burnt-feathers
and smelling-salts.
On that lamentable occasion hardly had the
vessel hove-to at Launceston than it was boarded
by an elderly matron and her pretty daughter,
the elderly intruder scurrying hither and thither
in search of “ My cousin, the Countess.”
Havingfound the Countess, this long-lost relative
proved her identity by handing in a faded paint-
ing labelled, “M Castle, the home of our
mutual great-grandmother.” The wily nobles
dissembled their agony and accepted the situation
by falling on their kinswoman’s neck and intro-
ducing her daughter to the suite, en masse. Before
parting they pledged themselves to afternoon tea
with their beaming Tasmanian cousin, who was
kind enough to promise that a photo, of “ M
Castle,” taken from the original masterpiece,
should commemorate the reunion. The day came,
the feast was spread, and the ladies, _ thoughtfully
arrayed in the family colours, waited for their
aristocratic connections until they realised that
the promises of “blood” are not a bit thicker
than water. Henceforth the demon of democracy
possessed this betrayed descendant of Celtic kings,
and she constantly dwelt upon the sins of her
great-grandmother’s line, beginning the catalogue
with “ Poverty ” and ending it with “ Pride.”
Governor Normanby (like Lord Jersey, who
once discovered a cousin of his in the proprietor of a
littleN.S. W. mountain saw-mill) was more tolerant
of obscure cousins. In Maoriland, when travelling
through a country district, he made a detour to
visit an unknown connection, who, having sunk
all his money in amateur fanning, was trying to
raise the wind by an experimental flour-mill.
The Marquis and suite found the miller as white
as Lot’s wife, owing to a hitch in the machinery,
which had necessitated burrowing through vats of
flour. He welcomed the nobility warmly, and
did his duty as a host, all day, in the same
farinaceous condition. Perhaps a bath, by trans-
forming this outer coating into a mass of dough,
would only have made matters worse.
Mrs. Paton, wife of New Hebrides Patom
whose letters were the best part of her husband’s
prosy “Life,” is writing a book on her own
account. She is a merry woman, in spite of her
husband, who weeps in and out of season. During
his recent visit to Canada the lachrymose mis-
sionary was called upon to identify a fraudulent
ex-parson, formerly of Melb., but just then await-
ing trial in the Dominion, for some characteristic
swindle.
As soon as Dr. Paton saw the culprit, he burst
into tears, as usual, exclaiming, “Lad ! lad ! but
I’m grieved to see you here!” “Not half so
much as I am to see you, Old Waterworks !” said
the black sheep. The inopportune arrival of an
Australian acquaintance had made his conviction
a certainty.
Herr Pinschoff, the originator of the “Credit
Foncier ” scheme (so far as Melb. is concerned) is
an authority on cuisine, as well as finance. In
his bachelor days he varied his billets-doux by en-
treating his talented fiancee to bring with her,
when she came to Australia to be married, every
cookery-book she could lay her hands on. She
obeyed with satisfactory results to all concerned.
When two eminent musicians (husband and wife)
visited us some time back, the enthusiastic Pin-
schoff testified his regard for genius by sending to
their hotel every day some chef d’oeuvre of national
cookery prepared at a Coll ins-street establishment
under his personal supervision.
According to a recent Bulletin paragraph,
“the daughters of a boss boomster, pupils at a
fashionable Melbourne college, are not likely to
encounter common people’s children, as, owing to
bad times, the latter are in most cases kept at
home to do the housework.” To make sure, how-
ever, “pa” enrolled the young ladies as “special
boarders,” engaging for their use a private sitting-
room, which even the teachers enter only by
invitation. Thus the wind is tempered to the
partially-shorn lambs.
One can now positively smell that Melbourne is
on the up-grade. “ Times-is-bad ” drove the one-
time smoker of fragrant Havanas to invest in vil-
lainous tobacco, the fumes from which rendered
suburban first-class railway carriages unbearable.
A man with a keen nasal organ tells me that he
can now detect the aroma of some of the finer
brands of tobacco, and that it is gradually growing
stronger.
A friend tells me that while visiting a small
Vic. township near Mount Macedon, the
other day, he attended a country funeral. The
horse was appropriately driven by a solemn man
with an inappropriately long red beard. The
vehicle stopped, and the coffin was conveyed
to the grave. “I looked for the clergyman, but
to my surprise the undertaker, who had retired to
the sexton’s house, then came forth attired in
clerical paraphernalia to read the prayers, after
which he disrobed and drove away. I now find he
is a licensed lay-reader, and acts as curate at
the parish church.” At this rate, we may yet
have a parson-undertaker acting also as doctor
and grave-digger.
The pilgrimage of the “Helping Hands” from
suburb to suburb is nearly accomplished—perhaps
none too soon. In the present cruel weather, the
springs of charity freeze hard, and the bonnie,
blithe, blink of oor ain fireside blinds one’s eyes to
the charms of peregrinating amateurs. Anyone
who patronises “ Helping Hands ” at a draughty
hall soon discovers that their mild sentimentali-
ties don’t tend to raise the circulation like
the methods of old professional hands. The
“spooney” passages in the gently dramati
sketch, “Dream Faces,” for instance, are marred
by the evident consciousness of the young pair,
that people are looking at them. The lady cast
for the innocent maiden titters constrainedly
where she should laugh naturally, while her lover,
who is shorter than his lass, never commits him-
self further than a waxed-moustache smile.
Mr. Innes-Noad is successful as a ne’er-do-well
father, but I think the sausage cut of his legs
would dissipate the last remnant of_ Aunt Mar-
garet’s love—if his butcher-boy winks hadn t
cured her sooner. A woman might condone the
riff-raffishness of her fallen hero, and shabby
gentility would probably awake her compassion ;
but “cheek,” under such circumstances, would
raise the dander of a saint.
Aunt Margaret, played by Mrs. Lewis, struck
one as too chastened for her age. Now that
grandmothers wear butterfly bonnets, a spinster
of 40 in perpetual mourning, with her hands
clasped, makes one impatient. The race of Aunt
Margarets is extinct.
At Max Hirsch’s lecture, the other night, the
chairman tried to prevent argument on the
grounds that the “ softer sex ” wished to catch the
10-o’clock train. Women like fair play, and apart
from that didn’t relish a fat man callingthem
“ the softer sex.” Mr. Zox, who gets no_ thinner,
was on the platform, and might just as fairly have
described us as the “plumper gender.”
At Melb. Women's Hospital, on Friday, the
ladies’ committee rebuked the matron for having a
picture representing kittens at play on one of the
walls. Evidently the committee aims at a mono-
poly of cattish things.
The N.S. Wales General Election.
At the time of writing, the election returns are
very incomplete, but the one fact which is abso-
lutely certain is that the present Protectionist
Government has been about as badly defeated as
any Government could well be. The old Free-
trade party—the men of the M'Millan revenue-
tariff persuasion—will evidently have a working
majority by themselves, and being able to dis-
pense with the support of the Labour element, all
necessity for preserving an outward semblance of
democratic sentiment has practically passed
away. The situation is, to all intents and
purposes, a return to the old days of the
Parkes regime, when land-tax and one-raan-one-
vote were not even dreamt of. Owing to the
woeful falling-off in the Protectionist party,
Labour will no longer hold the balance of power —
in fact, there is no balance to be held. The
National Ass wing of the Freetraders has secured
several seats, and this will probably stiffen the
backbone of the party of landlords and capi-
talists, and make it even less Democratic than
it was in the old days. A very large number of old
members vanish as the result of this election.
Suttor, Traill, Barton, Daniel O Connor and
Neild are among the lost; and of the Labour-
party, Cotton, Hindle, Kelly, and others
are missing. About the two biggest majorities
were those which swamped Traill and Cotton.
The aldermanic element will be very strong in
the new Parliament, which promises to be a
Legislature consisting principally of Parkes and
a vast collection of dull, conservative mediocrities.
The River Murray in its course falls 1
foot per mile; the Darling, 1 foot 2in.
The memorial services to the murdered

executed.
N.S. W. Beekeepers’ Association, in object-
ing to State school-teachers keeping bees, is on
the same industrial plane as the mat-makers who
object to prisoners making mats. There is in
both cases a surplus over personal use and that
surplus goes into competition with outside in-
dustry. The policeman’s wife who, out of his
wages, keeps three hens and manages to sell a
dozen eggs a week, is on directly the same footing
and should, as happened years ago in Victoria, be
suppressed if prisoners’ industry be suppressed.
Premier Patterson still maintains at the
perennial banquet, where he rises in evening dress
to pour out his full soul from above the turtle fat,
that there is very little poverty in Victoria, and
that the province is quite able to cope with any
that does exist. Yet every morning he can see
from his official window the police “dispersing”
the frost-bitten dead-beats from off the damp
grass in the Treasury Gardens, where they have
spent the night, and starting them off down the
street lest their presence should be a beastly
advertisement for the country. But perhaps
Patterson never looks out of the window.
The New Order, a Sydney Labour organ,
strikes an important truth :
Attorney-General Heydon holds that missing word com-
petitions are illegal, and has notified promoters to dis-
continue them. Whenever Labour men or Radicals
technically “infringe the law’’ they are never notified by
a kindly disposed Attorney-General— but when it’s the
proprietors of the sixpenny Bulletin and twopenny
Tkijtii there you are, don’t yer know 1
Truly, there you are. When The Bulletin broke
the law by running a missing word competition it
received a polite intimation from the Attorney-
General’s department that it must leave oft doing
it. When Mr. Andrews broke the law by
circulating a harmless pamphlet with a technically
deficient imprint, he didn’t receive any polite in-
timation from the Attorney-General; he simply
received three months. Moreover, Mr. Andrews
obviously offended from pure ignorance, whereas
The Bulletin didn’t offend from ignorance, and
didn’t even pretend to. Therefore, as the New
Order eloquently remarks, “ there you are.”
The Victorian Boom-Its Price in Crime.
In one of bis books Marion Oraweord tells
how, at a certain period of the year, the reindeer,
calmly grazing in their northern pastures, begin
to exhibit symptoms of unrest, which speedily
expand to an uncontrollable'excitement, and,
turning their faces towards the ocean, the herd
with one accord commence their journey at a
gallop over the hundreds of miles that may in-
tervene, and continue without pause until they
reach the confines of the ocean. Then, having
tasted of the salt sea, they return peacefully to
their wonted pastures. Any one who has to do
with large bodies of men finds fitful examples of
like unreason. Soldiers become restless and
insubordinate, workmen discontented, scholars
unmanageable. Of this spirit the prisons afford
the most striking examples. With scarce any
warning murmurs of discontent, prisoners break
out in open mutiny, destroy their clothing and
other belongings, assault their keepers, and
give general evidence of temporary madness,
which subsides more rapidly even than it arose.
* * •
Nor is the general population exempt from
similar disturbances, which appear so un-
accountable on any ordinary principles that
writers of good repute have endeavoured to ex-
plain the phenomenon by supposing some
external physical forces operate on human
affairs. Lyon Playfair formulates a theory of
economic cycles to account for periods of in-
dustrial activity and depression. These cycles
are not the direct product of human agency, and
are not far removed in character from the re-
current periods of disturbance observable in
social life. N.S.W. astronomer Russell
postulates, with some show of reason, a nineteen
years’ periodicity of rain, depending on the size
of the sun-spots; and Statistician Johnston, of
Tasmania, has a theory of light and heavy
mortality dependent on the movement of
Jupiter’s satellites and other planetary eccen-
tricities. To whatever cause they may be due,
there are social as well as industrial ferments
which seem to occur at regular periods. Now,
the impulse puts on the aspect of religion and
is called a revival, now of commercial madness
and is called a boom, and as often as not coinci-
dent with the other two, the social distension is
marked by an eruption of crime as hideous as
unaccountable. Of the three, the last is the
most interesting.
* • *
The late boom, here in Victoria, to which we
attribute all our woes, was fairly under way in
1887, and may be considered as having spent
its force within five years. To illustrate the
business in hand, it will not be unprofitable to
compare the five years which closed with 188 G
with the five years from 1887 to 1891, during
which the boom raged strongly. No one can very
well trace any necessary connection between the
boom and murder and other blood crimes.
That murders should be common in times
closely following a boom is a thing easy to be
understood. But what are the facts ? In the
year before the boom the arrests for murder
and other crimes involving the shedding of
blood had a yearly average of just over 100 ; in
the boom period they numbered on the average
134 —in one year, indeed, the arrests were 151 ;
since the smash, strange to say, the average
has dropped to less than 100. Assaults on
females increased greatly during the boom, but
have since fallen below their former average.
Forgery rose from 70 a year to 110. Drunken-
ness and disorder showed a yearly score of
20,200 arrests before the boom, compared with
26,700 during its continuance. Assaults on the
police averaged 820, compared witli 950—the
present rate is below 700. Violent attacks on
property rose from 250 to 420, other offences
against property from 3200 to 4000, and violent
assaults from 1100 to 1280.
# # #
What is also very curious about this saturnalia
of crime is the pause for breath in 1889, about
the middle of the boom. Crime did not fall
altogether to its normal level, but offences of
all kinds were much less frequent than in the
two preceding and the two subsequent years.
It would be an excellent thing if it w’ere pos-
sible to place all this bloodshed, riot, debauchery,
lust and general roguery to the discredit of
Jupiter’s satellites, but if we do so we must
place amongst the satellites the Melbourne
papers one and all, the Matthew Davieses, the
banks, the Larkinses, and the Government
itself, all having striven with equal earnestness
to augment the social madness. No one has yet
made out a balance-sheet of the boom. When
it is prepared—if it is accurate—it will have to
the debit; 170 murders, 1500 violent assaults,
100 serious crimes against women, 850 bur-
glaries, and 4000 other raids on property, 200
cases of forgery, 32,500 of riotous drunkenness,
all within five years—offences which would
never have been committed had there been no
boom.

What has become of Madame Midas ?
The wife of a N.S. Wales clergyman has
Sapphira for a first name.
In London they describe Phil May’s
appearance as that of “a monk masquerading as
a bookmaker.”
People will be sorry to hear that a very
prominent N.S.W. politician’s illness is believed
to be heart-disease.
Seven reporters have gone from the Age
staff during the past few months. David Syme
has Speight preying on his liver.
The well-beloved Landseer, M.P., says
the farmers of S.A. “have been scratching their
heads for years to find a solution of the vermin
difficulty.”
Of the eight lady doctors in Melbourne,
three are named Stone. Dr. Constance Stone was
the first “native” lady doctor to practise in the
city by the Yarra.
In last N.S.W. Parliament, of 141 mem-
bers were 81 who resided permanently in Sydney
and its suburbs, leaving 60 to keep house over the
rest of the territory.

G. Hell, Corowa; Earnest Anxiety Traynor, pub‘
liean, Moree; C. C. Mi hell, vendor of religious
literature, Summer Hill.

dates, comedian George Coppin once, having

tions the B.Sc. degree was taken by a young
Sydneyite of 20, wi th first-class honours. He was

lady” shortly if Frank aforesaid can manage it,
is daughter of gold-commissioner Hawkins, who

of the Melb. Union official of that ilk, and niece

Parkes’ father, at 70, was an absolutely
hairless man, without even the semblance of a
whisker to shelter him from public criticism.
Evidently, Henry didn’t leave any hair for the
rest of the family.
Bishop Kennion’s appointment to the see
of Bath and Wells makes the third of such trans-
fers from Australasia to England. Bishop Moor-
house to Manchester and Bishop Selwyn to Lich-
field were similar promotions.
That ex-Ageut-General Bray’s mental con-
dition was much exaggerated is proved by the
fact that less than a week before he died he wrote,
signed, addressed and posted at Aden a perfectly
coherent letter to editor Bonython, of Adelaide.
His object was to catch the Messageries boat,
which was expected to beat the Oceana by a day
in the run to Adelaide.
The mispronuncia-
tion of the word, “Moran”
causes much indignation
amongst Hibernians. A
story in point: Bishop
Moran, of Maori land,
had a parrot which used
to overhear the enquiries
of callers, many of whom
(non-Catholics) gave a
wrong construction of
the Bishop’s name. One
day the parrot was heard
assiduously repeating—
“ Bishop M’ran, Bishop
M’ran, Bishop M’ran—
no, Bishop Mawran,
Bishop Mawran, Bishop
Mawran. Quite correct,
quite correct.”
A review editor,
writing from London to
an Australian pressman,
remarked lately that the
Sladenesque analogies
which Australian writers
institute ■ between their
countrymen and eminent
men of elsewhere, have
a tendency to prejudice
people against Austra-
lians altogether. As if to
illustrate, he stated that
he once received an
article from Australia,
which afterwards reached
print in a London penny
paper, wherein the writer
referred to a Mr. John
Osborne as the Danton of
Australian letters, to a
Mr. Curnow as the
Thucydides, to a Mr.
Drayton as the Rabelais,
and to a Mr. Hogue as the
Mirabeau of antipodean
literature. The review
man added: “Have you
no animals or ancestors
of your own to liken your
great men to ?”

The old offices of “ Bob ” Nichols, in Pitt-
street, Sydney, where “Jimmy ” Martin (N.S.W.
Chief Justice) early in life dusted musty law-
books, cut his initials into the legal furniture,
and subsequently served his articles, were vacated
recently, and the time-honoured relics removed
to a brand-new building.

inst., and he loses thereby several thousands
sterling.

settler, and one of the first aldermen of Albury
(N.S.W.), died on I3th inst., aged 86 years; and
Michael O’Shea, for half-a-eentury a settler in the
Wellington district, N.S.W., died on 14th inst.,
aged 96 years.
Had Cummins, to be hanged at Tamworth
on 20th inst., not been convicted of the Barraba
murder, he would have probably been among the
candidates at the N.S.W. general elections. As it
is, he stands on the hangman’s hustings, asking for
the suffrages of God.
Kemp, the Vic. civil servant who got five
years for embezzlement, was confident that he
would receive a light sentence, and at a football-
match, on the Saturday before his trial, he said
he expected about 12 months. Then the faithful
crowd drank to his speedy liberation.
Cardinal Moran (N.S.W.) says that none
of the party leaders have been able to discover
whether he is a freetrader or a protectionist. The
Bulletin categorises him as—“nayther, and
ayther, and both.” Nayther, politically ; ayther,
educationally; and both, in blended sense.
J. M. Barrie, the novelist, is reported to
be dangerously ill in London, and the doctors are
said to have little hope of his recovery. Yet that
other novelist, Isaiah Reginald Cohen, of Sydney,
continues in robust health. Truly poetic justice
is only a dream and the shadow of a shade.
Apropos medical officers in lunatic asylums
becoming mentally shaky. The resident med.
in the chief asylum of a southern province was
lately examined by leading doctors, who have
confidentially informed Govt, that he is insane.
The officer is now on leave prior to retirement.
The just-demised N.S.W. squatter Rand
was one of bushranger Morgan’s victims. Morgan
made him put up a notice on bis gate: “All
travellers welcome.” After Morgan was shot,
down came the notice. The travellers came. Says
Rand ; “ Eh, but yer freend’s gone the noo.” Yet
Rand was not a mean man.
Kaahu, a Maori chief said to have been
120 years old, died at Turakina (M.L.) on June
18. Nearly a century ago, when the Waikato
tribes warred against Kaahu’s tribe, he, with
others, was captured. He was a slave with the
Waikatos for over 50 years, but 40 years ago he
was permitted to make his way back to his friends.
Which of the Australised sons of Charles
Dickens is the young man for whom the novelist
obtained a business-appointment in China? The
story goes that the son in question was bon-
voyaged by his parent and a distinguished com-
pany at a fine family dinner, and the guest of the
evening carried out the tradition of such occasions
by becoming elevated and noisy. Then father
Charles, in his best manner, suggested that, as
the boy was anxious to gain Chinese experience,
“ a little transaction in tea ” would be desirable.

J. L. PURVES. Q.C.
Mrs. Keirnan, of Albury (N.S.W.), who
died on Friday, 13th inst., aged 96 years, was
supposed to be the oldest native of the province,
having been born at Prospect, near Parramatta,
in 1798. She married the late Dr. Keirnan,
the medical superintendent of the first Australian
immigrant ship, and leaves innumerable descen-
dants.
A. Alston, who won the Yic. National
Gallery Travelling Scholarship of 1890 with his
picture, “Flood Sufferings,” is now doing some
clever black-and-white brush drawings in the
London weeklies. Rupert Bunny (it is not he,
by the way, but his brother George who is the
barrister of the family) seems to be the only other
Australian artist who oan tear himself away from
colour; his work in the French magazines is
simple, pretty and effective.
Our recent visitor, the Duchess of Buck-
ingham, in her “Glimpses of Four Continents,”
speaks of “the portrait of the Earl of Hopetoun
in his hat” as very effective. The Duchess, who
is to be married shortly to a sexagenarian peer, is
chiefly remarkable as the wearer of the biggest
spectacles ever seen off the nose of a Chinese shop-
keeper.
LADY HOPETOUN.
Mr. J. R. Fairfax, chief proprietor of
S.M. Herald, has reprinted for private circula-
tion one of the addresses of his father, the late
John Fairfax. The address was originally
delivered nearly 40 years ago, and contains among
a number of profound sentiments the following
insufficiently-realised truth : “It is a solemn thing
to be a man.”
The Argus has heard from poor old John
Nimmo, ex-M.L.A. for South Melbourne, snuffle-
buster, teetotal crank, Premier Permanent boom-
ster, and once Minister of the Crown. John prays
that “all the good, honest people of Victoria may
be protected from land-sharks, scare-mongers and
wreckers.” Nimmo is living in some out-of-the-
way Scotch hamlet, whence he departed when the
Premier Permanent burst, and his associate,
Mirams, was gaoled. The announcement that he
does not intend returning to Australia proves the
genuineness of his prayer.
Andrew MTlwraith, of jMTlwraith,
M’Eacharn & Co., was lately in Rockhampton.
MTlwraith was really the father of the frozen
meat industry, inasmuch as he despatched the
first cargo of frozen meat to London, per s.s.
Strathmore, in 1880. Singularly enough MTl-
wraith resides at St. Albans, the birthplace and
residence of Francis Bacon, who fell a victim to
his desire to achieve what MTlwraith has suc-
cessfully accomplished. It will be remembered
that the former died from a heavy cold contracted
during chilling experiments.
A connecting-link between past and pre-
sent Sydney passed away on Sunday in the person
of Mrs Isabella Long, relic of that wealthy old-
time identity, “ Bill ” Long. Mrs. Long was the
mother of three daughters, two of whom wedded
men who have made a mark in the history of N.S.
Wales. The eldest in ’53 married the doughty
James Martin, solicitor, afterwards Attorney-
General and Premier, and finally Chief Justice.
Another married the “shirt-sleeved Caesar,”
W. B. Dailey, who became Attorney-General,
and (by reason of his Soudan expedition) a Privy
Councillor, and who could have been Chief Justice
had he chosen. The third married George Cheeke,
nephew and Associate to Mr. Justice Cheeke, of
racing fame. The only son of the lady just
deceased is Mr, W. A. Long, M.L.0., sometime
N.S.W. Colonial Treasurer. Thus “Old Bill”
loved law and politics, having wedded hisdaughters
to the profession and put his son also to the Bar.
The latter, however, has never practised, being
content with the great wealth inherited from his
father.
The chairman at a West Macquarie (N.S.
Wales) election meeting, the other day, intro-
duced W. H. Marriott, a candidate, in these
terms: —“Blokes, we’re yero ter-night ter yere
Marriott. Ye all know him. He’s a farmer
somewheres—a tiller er the soil. On lookin’
around at this yere push assembled, I carn't find
a face as intelligent as his’n.”
The Hon. Berkeley, the present Chief
Justice of Fiji, is very strict and differs vastly
from former ones. The most popular Chief Judge
Fiji ever had was the most eccentric. A stranger
at the Courthouse dared not laugh or express sur-
prise if he should observe his Honor, when deciding
some knotty Coolie case, calmly remove his wig
and place it carefully on his water-bottle. His
first session, it is said, he appeared one morning
with a beard on one side of his face for the clerk
to decide which style he should adopt in order to
meet the exigencies of the climate.
W. J. Tarplee, the veteran Sydney press-
man, ex-chief reporter of the S.M. Herald, who
died last week, appointed himself the providence
at different periods, of three or four of the most
unmanageable pressmen of Sydney. He lectured
them when they did badly, extolled them when
they did well, and was often poorer in pocket on
their account. He drifted into Government em-
ployment in the hope of one day receiving the
appointment of editor of that incongruous pro-
duction, History prom the Records. Tarplee
was the sort of man who goes to Heaven when he
dies, for he had not an enemy in the world, not
even himself.
I DON’T LIKE TO TALK OF MYSELF.
“I’ve little to say of myself.” (Parkes* opening sen I enee
as reported in Review of Reviews.) The pronoun “I”
then occurs just 20 times in 20 lines.
I was born in a cottage at ’Ome,
I deserted that cottage to roam ;
I was young (in my childhood) and gay,
I was fond of the gals in my day ;
I was handsome and tall as a Samson or Saul,
And I’ve little to say of myself.
I am eighty, ye scoffers, to-day.
And a hearty old buck in my way;
I’m a Premier, a knight, and a poet,
And what I don’t pay—why, I owe it;
I’m devoid of all guile, as you see by my smile,
And I’ve little to say of myself.
I once was a Radical red.
But now I’m a Radical dead ;
I am fond of the lay and the lyric,
Of the Empire and all things Empiric ;
I am modest and shy, as you see by my eye,
And I’ve little to say of myself.
From the plains of the West to the sea
You’ll hear ’em a-talking of Me—
Of Me and My eloquence mellow,
My poems on Me and My Stella ;
I could tell you much more, but I mentioned
before
I don’t like to talk of myself.
Q-
P. L.
Purves.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
No Australian barrister keeps quite so much in
evidence as James Liddell Purves, Q.C. He is
always the foremost man in Melbourne’s foremost
case. His ability is most versatile—no man living
is more prompt in receiving impressions and
gleaning the knowledge immediately useful to the
advocate.
Mr.Purves, born in Victoria, began hiseducation
at Melb. Diocesan Grammar School, a little brick
place, near St. Peter’s Church, under the ferule of
Mr. Budd, afterwards chief of an important
ladies’ seminary. His schoolmates there included
Box, the barrister, and Harry Budd, the cricket-
ing and theatrical attorney, while Vicar Hand-
held, now of St. Peter’s, was a kind of junior
master. Purves’ father was a wealthy but
eminently luckless racing man, and but for this
hereditary kink Purves would never have eared
two straws for the turf—so he is as unlucky as his
father was.
Like Dick Ireland and Aspinall, he is no lawyer
■ —from an expert’s view-point. Campbell said of
Brougham, “He would know everything, if he
only knew a little law,’’ and yet lie made an
excellent Chancellor, even as Purves would make
a better Judge than Tubby Kerferd, or possibly
as good a one as Hartley Williams. However, he
is not likely to be one—the job would bore him.
Where Purves excels all is in grasping and
analysing technicalities —whether in surgery,
medicine, soft-goods, sea-craft, mining, engineer-
ing, or whatever may turn up, and proving that
the 30,000 volumes of law-books in the Supreme
Court Library are as entirely off the matter, of
any practical consideration, as was Dogberry’s
statement of the case to Leonato.
Purves is never ill, and never well. Men built
thus must be excused for being irritable and
tetchy, and their hearts may be wrongly blamed
for their stomachs, livers, or kidneys. Shelley
said the “ Cenci” would never have been such a
fine play but for the horrible pain he endured
while writing it. Give Purves a good pain—his
favourite one is a cobbler stitching at his side—
and you will have a good, bludgeony speech. His
malaise may be read in his smileless and soulless
visage, but no man is more beloved by his friends,
for he is staunch and true, shaping up for some
such a character as the Tory Lord Eldon, of whom
the mob shouted “Cheer him; he never ratted.”
When the new Courts were opened, luxurious,
wadded chairs were provided. Purves insisted on
having a cane one for himself.
Like Syme, S. Y. Winter, and other ferocities,
Purves is kind-hearted. A genuine appeal to his
mercy will spoil his cross-examination—and so
will a bold defiance. Otherwise he undoubtedly
sails very close to the bullying line, in
a thorough identification of himself with
the convictions and feelings of his client.
This is not with malice prepense—it is uncon-
scious, and he is inclined to weep if any
club crony tells him he has been cruel. Good
actors nowadays are termed convincing, and here
is forensic Purves—he puts on his cause like a
well-fitting surtout. He “goes blind.” It is an
elephant crashing through the jungle, a Jugger-
naut oar driven on, with its thick wooden wheels,
over any number of carcasses.
He is intense in his fondness of home. His only
drawback there is that he never can shake off his
cases ; go where he will, he always has one,
only one, on his mind, brooding over him, rather
than he over it. Joe Thompson, on the night
before the battle of the Melbourne Cup, was not
more wakeful than Purves is on the eve of launch-
ing a great case, or summing it up—as he is accus-
tomed to tell his juries. It is an octopus,
clasping him round. No one will say his powers
are truly great. Other men, his rivals, could have
done as much, if they were only enabled to have
such a feeling of their business, able to imbue
themselves with their case, live for a day, or a
week, or a month, the very life of their client, as
with an Avatar.
His club is the Athenaeum. Matt. O’Shanassy
is the man he most misses, and he gets on admi-
rably with Harrie Smith, and all those of turfy
flavour, although he really does not care much for
the turf. Popping at pigeons is his offhand little
hobby. He will dabble in billiards and any card
game you like, but never can detach his mind for
any hearty pleasure—always triste, morne, sar-
donic : a mixture of the most awkward points of
the English and German. He has been twice
married, and has a family.

Let the Victorian civil servants beware
that they do not lose their votes.
“Queensland capitalists have all the vir-
tues of capitalists generally, except the possession
of capital.” — J. G. Drake.
Ballina, N.S.W., electorate, with some-
where about 2500 names on the roll, has only four
persons described as “ gentlemen.”
Current American events bring to memory
the devices for selling the Australian State rail-
ways. If we sell the railways, we buy a civil war.
When Sydney Daily Worker quotes the
phrases of “a great Australian, the late Chief
Justice Martin,” it should remember that that
great Australian was a cast-iron Protectionist.
Balaam Epps, secretary of N.S. Wales
National Ass., dates his official bulls from
“Aurum Chambers.” “Aurum”— “gold.” Gold
—the colour of carrots. Carrots —the lure of the
Asa.
“ If there is one thing I have to be thank-
ful for more than another, it is that daring my
public life I have saved myself from the disgrace
of ever having been a colleague of Henry Parkes.
—G. H. Reid.

Maoriland Upper House, which still con-
tains many rich, full-bodied nominees, has
violently shocked the Cold Tea-party by vetoing
the Lower House’s resolution to close the bar of
the Parliamentary refreshment-room.
Sydney publicans, particularly those carry-
ing on business near Parliament House, are,
according to a prominent N.S.W. local optionist,
the most energetic workers in the movement to
abolish the local Parliamentary liquor bar.
The Royal Tar, after carrying a cargo of
coal from Newcastle to Honolulu, will go to Puget
Sound for a load of timber for Melbourne. Ihe
New Australians wishful to return to the old
home will apparently have to wait a long time for
their ark.
Dr. J. W. Barrett, lecturing at Melbourne
on “Government by Popular Clamour,” argued
that the history of civilisation shows that the
dissolution of States has always “commenced
from the time of the full extension of the franchise
to the people.” Now, no State has ever tried
“ the full extension of the franchise to the
people.”

One of the constantly-
repeated gags among the
daily rot of electioneering
candidates is that “the in-
troduction of female suffrage
will raise the moral tone of
politics.” The men who
mouth this are either the
most arrant among humbugs,
or else they are political
Rosicrucians, who waste for-
tunes of energy over an
imaginary “philosopher’s
stohe.” The naked, brutal
truth is—“ There is no
‘moral tone’in politics ” to
be either lowered or raised.
And, as long as an upper
rung in political circles
means power and easy, if
sinister, means to acquire
pelf, a “ moral tone ” will
never be born into the poli-
tical drum.

South Australia is about to cut deeply into
its civil service salaries; all public officers, except
the Judges, are to lose 25 per cent, of the sum
they receive in excess of £6OO yearly. The first
£l5O is exempt, the next .£l5O pays 6 percent.,
the next £3OO pays 12h per cent., and above that
sum as before stated. Thus the Agent-General
comes down from £l5OO a year to £1230, and the
Chairman of Railway Commissioners from £2500
to £1990.
F. M. Stone, a Westralian Legislative
Council candidate: —
1 am opposed to one man one vote.
1 am opposed to payment of members.
I am opposed to a land-tax.
I believe in the stock-tax (which raises the price of meat
for the benefit of the squatter).
I would support a bonus for the best clip of wool.
I am in favour of coloured labour.
As a specimen of the insolence of a landed aristo-
cracy this is hard to beat.

Griffiths’ (Q.) sentence of nine months’
hard, awarded to the “seditious ” Mount Morgan
crank, lately cropped up in Maoriland Parliament.
One loyal Labour member thought the sentence
well-deserved, and hoped everybody who took the
Queen’s name in vain would get as much. Then
said Radical O'Regan: “And yet, on the gold-
fields, I have heard men go even farther, and take
the Almighty’s name in vain ; but I cannot remem-
ber that any of them got nine months’ gaol for
thus insulting the highest of potentates.” Queens
are afraid of being abolished. God isn’t.

electorate of Nannine (the Murchison), formerly

clever talker and sound democrat Premier
Forrest did his best to keep Illingworth out; and
his nominee, Darldt, an old resident squatter, lost
the fight on his principles rather than on personal
grounds However, the Government has deep
satisfaction in the defeat of De Hamel, a per-
sistent thorn in its side, and formerly member
for Albany, who this year offered himself for
Yilgarn (Coolgardie). The miners preferred C.
Moran, formerly connected with Q. Labour-
party ; but De Hamel will be much missed.

A while ago Jenkin Coles, the newly-
knighted S.A. Speaker, waxed wrath at the
immorality of land-value taxation. Then a cor-
respondent unearthed the following, the senti-
ments of plain Jenkin Coles, in 1884
The men we want to reach are those who owned city
acres that cost them a comparatively small sum of money,
but at the present moment were worth thousands of
pounds. In many cases these people had done nothing
vatfhLteln 0 increased
neighbours, and their owners reaped the benefit of it. To
those people we should say— You must pay a tax on the
actual value of your land. We do not wish to tax any
improvements; we wish to encourage these, but you
must contribute to the State in proportion to the in-
creased value of your land.

A new naval commandant due about the same

before the century closes, anyway, and natives are
to take charge of the Artillery and Engineer
departments upon the retirement of the Imperial
officers now in possession.

M Millan would refuse to join any N.S._W.

now despise on account of his twistings and

business during the maritime strike. In some

An official report on insanity in Ireland
declares that much of the neurotic disease and
madness prevalent in that country is due to the
continued inordinate use of tea and tobacco.
This is quite believable. Every reader of this
par. must know several teetotallers who are subject
to recurrent fits of D. Tea., and are ordinarily
“as mad as hatters,” Will some penological
statistician please tell for what percentage of
criminality in Australia tea-drinking is respon-
sible ? The subject is a fearsomepne, and suggests
the necessity of legislation against tea-swilling.
Afternoon tea awakens the malice of the gentlest
women, and makes assassins of them. Post-and-
rail tea numbers its bush loonies by thousands.
Hash-house tea is innocuous, because it mainly
consists of floor-sweepings and boiled leaves that
have been sun-dried again, and curled up by the
oozing perspiration of Chinamen. There s death
and Beelzebub in the family tea-pot, and happy is
he who abjures tea.

Cairns Akgus regrets that 50 Chinese
gardeners are leaving Cairns for the Herbert to
plant eane. Poor Cairns ! The day fast' comes
when its white inhabitants will come, cap in hand,
to a pig-tailed mayor for a job at planting cab-

America looks ugly. Monopoly of land
and accumulation of wealth are possible up to a
certain point; but America is not like Eng-
land, where an army can be concentrated on any
given point in 48 hours. The strength of the
American army is based on the supposition that it
will have to deal with a people peaceful and law-
abiding. Even a minority of the citizens, if very
much in earnest, can make things highly unplea-
sant for the Government. Let Australia look to
it that her own systems of government do not
repeat the errors which in America drive men to
social revolt.
Editor Bulletin, —In your issue of this
date you state that the Rev. T. B. Tress “ received
an offer of <£6oo and residence from Richmond,
Yic.” As that statement is not true, I must ask
you, in the interest of Mr. Tress, to correct it. The
stipend first offered was >£4oo (not .£600), which
was afterwards increased to £500; but this
amount, from several causes, such as,the different
arrangement that exists in Melbourne as to fees,
the difference in the cost of living, and from
having to leave some members of his family in
Sydney, is not only no inducement but actually
places Mr. Tress in a rather worse financial posi-
tion than he was in Sydney.—l am yours, &c. t
Wm. H. Cbago, L.R.C.P., London, &c., 31 Col
lege-street, Hyde Park, Sydney, July 14, 1894.
I am Tired !
Sufferers from indigestion and liver complaint at®
subject to a continued feeling of drowsiness, head-
ache, tired aching limbs, and a general inclination
to languor. These symptoms are due primari y
to a deficient secretion of bile, the natural cathartic
of the liver, which, when secreted in insufficient
quantities, permits the coarse and fibrous particles
of the food and other waste and poisonous matter
from all parts of the system to become
impacted in the duodenem and lower intestines,
creating a very dangerous condition which requires
instant and proper treatment. An experience of
five years with Clements Tonic has proved this
remedy a specific for such complaints, and its
universal success has resulted in thousands of
imitations being forced on the market; not because
they possess any therapeutic value, for they are
purely speculative ventures. Sufferers who desire
to get relief must insist on having Clements Tonic.
It acts scientifically, has proved its efficacy in
thousands of cases as effectually as in that of Mr.
C. L. Carter, Queanbeyan, N.S.W., who writes
on May 23rd, 1893 :—I have used your Clements
Tonic with the moat beneficial results. I have
been a severe sufferer for some considerable time
from dyspepsia; I have tried various remedies
without receiving any relief. Hearing so much
about the wonderful cures performed by your
Clements Tonic I resolved to try it, and am happy
to say it done me a lot of good. I can with con-
fidence recommend it to any suffering from dys-
pepsia.—C. L. Carter, Queanbeyan, N.S.W.

i Jwo more Chinese arrived in Hobson’s
Bay yesterday by the steamer Fitzroy from
Sj'daey. The vessel being only 870 tons
burthen, the Customs authorities calculated
that she was only entitled to land one and
three-quarter Chinamen under the tonnage

diminutive stature, and it was ingeniously

A will has just turned up, near Melbourne,
dated 1874, which, had it been discovered 19 years
ago, would have been good enough to base a
romance upon. The maker died in 1875, and
under the only will found (dated 1872) disin-
herited her son in favour of her own housekeeper.
The son shot himself, after unsuccessfully trying
to upset the 1872 will, whilst the housekeeper
died from alcoholic poisoning, eight years later,
in Melbourne gaol, without a copper. Under the
later will the son would have got <£9ooo in cash
and a furnished house.
* # #
Mdlle. Gdnot, the French lady with the
baggy pants, is pursuing her way through Maori-
land, and her devastating path can be traced by
the paragraphs in the local press. The Maori-
land editors, as a rule, make a great effort to take
the bifurcated visitor seriously, but they are
evidently badly floored by the back view of
mademoiselle when she turns to leave the office.
This isn’t surprising, for there is a shapeless and
cow-like aspect about that back view which is
sufficiently alarming. This is especially the case
when the lady stoops down, under which conditions
sheappears likeagrey feather-bed with boots on. In
Christchurch, where the local girl habituaby rides
a bicycle in a neat knickerbocker suit, the form-
less bagginess of the French lady seems to have
been taken as a personal insult.
# # #
Dr. Reunion, Anglican Bishop of Adel-
aide, after a very weak wrestle with the spirit,
has accepted the Bishopric of Bath and Wells at
£5OOO p.a., and as his Adelaide post was only
worth £2OOO a very short wrestle was sufficient.
Dr. Kennion is far from popular in S. A., indeed
the ladies disliked him because he once made ill-
judged remarks about their insincerity as wor-
shippers. His clergy will not be sorry when he
leaves, as on his recent return from England he
commanded them to shave, with the result that
many clerical faces, their “hardness” hitherto
hidden by beards, bore mute testimony that there
was much in the Darwinian theory.
Dr. Kennion has been angling for an English
bishopric for some time, and it was doubtful if he
would return from England on his recent visit,
but “my Lord” denied that he wanted to leave
Australia. Mrs. Kennion is a sister of Sir James
Fergusson, formerly a S.A. Governor, who
perhaps had a little to do with the present
appointment. The bishop is a confirmed Tory
and will be in his element preaching to decrepit
dowagers and decayed dukes at Bath and Wells.
• • *
There is joy in Cooktown. A new
Anglican diocese is to be created embracing
northern parts from Cooktown to Port Darwin, and
the southern coast of New Guinea. The grouping
of North Queensland and Southern New Guinea
is a neat piece of sarcasm on the part of the
church authorities. The new diocese will pro-
bably be the most mongrel-ridden district ever
mapped off by sacred or secular authorities.

« * «
The fashionable Collins-street barber
caused a block last week with his window show.
A young lady sat inside the window with her back
to it. She was wrapped up in professional style,
while adown her back was the most superb long
wavy auburn hair ever seen off a tricopherous
advt. As she gently moved her head, it suggested
the slow unfolding of a gorgeous boa-constrictor’e
back.
• * «
Melbourne snobs cannot understand that
in the republic of Art a Governor is not necessarily
of any greater consequence that a street musician.
A sill}' groveller who moved a vote of thanks to
Gov. Robinson after the production of the latter’s
opera had the cool impudence to say that H.E.’s
music had all the lightness of Auber and the
French school, with all the delicacy and fanciful-
ness of Arthur Sullivan. Why not all the
grandeur of Wagner, too, and all the sublimity of
Beethoven, and all the beauty of Mozart, and all
the marvellousness of Marshall Hall ?
By the way, when the opera aforesaid was per-
formed before the “select” and invited Melb.
audience, a particular request was made that the
ladies and gentlemen would return the books of
the words at the conclusion. The ladies and
gentlemen at once commenced to smuggle their
copies into their muffs and coat-pockets, and not
half the books were returned.

The W.C.T. Union recently complained
piteously to the Melb. Police Department that
highly indecent pictures were being exhibited in a
certain city hotel. An intelligent constable was
told off to investigate, with the result that he
reported the engravings cavilled at to he unobjec-
tionable works of art —they were nothing more,
in fact, than Psyche at Eleusis and the “ Daphne-
phoria.” What nasty minds these nice people
have!
# * #
A few points for mean men ; Reverse your
hat periodically, wearing it back to front on alter-
nate weeks. Then it is twice as long before the
perspiration-mark in front makes it unwearable.
Never be bow-legged, which is wanton extra-
vagance. The bow-legged man wears the outer
side of his boot-sole, which is its weakest part;
the economical knock-kneed man wears the inner
side, which lasts much longer. Always carry
half-sovereigns; never sovereigns. If you pay
anybody with a sovereign he may give you change
for a half-sov. in spite of your remonstrances ; if
you always give a half-sov., some day or other
somebody will give you change for a whole one
by mistake. If you carry notes always use those
of the most obscure and shaky bank going.
Very often, in panic times, after you
have had a drink or a lunch, the pro-
prietor flatly refuses to take the note of
the shaky little bank. Then you tell him you
have no change and owe hinuthe money. Never
use receipt-stamps. When you sign a receipt put
on the bottom of it a square patch of wet just the
size of a stamp, and let the ends of your name
appear on each side of the damp vacancy. If
this is neatly done, it is apparent that the stamp
was there, but has unfortunately fallen off. Don’t
wear a moustache. The clean-shaved man can
smoke a cigar down to the last quarter-inch, while
the man with a moustache has to sacrifice at least
an inch else his lip ornament will be conflagrated
and the flames will proceed up his nose.
* * *
The Duchess of Buckingham, whose en-
gagement to Lord Egerton of Tatton has just
been announced, is a cousin of Governor Hope-
toun. Her determination to re-marry recalls the
saying that “ second marriages are the triumph of
hope over experience.” When she was in Australia
she was chiefly noted for her genial ways and
for her skill in looking at least a decade younger
than her years. Her handsome presence was en-
hanced by a rollicking manner which, on dit,
allowed her to enter into the fun of a pillow-
fight on the mail-boat. In fact, Ann Buckingham,
nee Graham Montgomery, was in every respect
“ awfully jolly.”
• • •
There is a Melb. man who, some years
ago, when his wife died, erected a larger tomb-
stone over the grave in place of the diminutive
one which previously bore the name of their child.
He tried to get a substantial rebate for the return
of the old slab, but being offered only 15s. he
concluded to take it home and use it as a “ paste-
board.” Which he did.

Henry Butler, of Melbourne, who not
long ago drew the <£30,000 prize in the Queens-
land property lottery, has had another run of
luck. He has now drawn a prize in the matri-
monial lottery. His fiancee is a 20-year old blonde,
Miss Wilson, daughter of Mr. J. A. Wilson, of
the Bijou Theatre and Palace Hotel proprietary.
Mr. Butler is a partner in the softgoods firm, of
which Mr. Sargood is the most ornate member.
He is a genial soul of three-score.

funds of a charitable institution, to see himself in
print in that connection.

“Advanced” teetotallers are advocating
that spirit and beer-sellers should not be admitted
to church membership in Australia. But if the
sellers are run out, the manufacturers must also
be excluded. Hence the churches won’t go with
these reformers.

During the recent N.S.W. election there
appeared in the window of a Sydney assignation
house the placard of a certain Freetrade candidate
with the words: “Vote for . He’s the
Man for Us.” “Us” in this case evidently meant
the tenants of the premises, and when one con
eiders the kind of man who would be the man for
them, and the reasons why he would be that man,
this kind of enthusiasm seems rather rough on the
candidate. It doesn’t help any man on his way
to Parliament to have it publicly announced that
he is cordially approved of in such quarters. It is
as bad as being secretly adopted as a candidate,
without your leave, by the National Ass.

Dear Bulletin, —Does not a ship illus-
trate well the relation of man and woman ! Man
and woman are parts of the same vessel, “jour-
neying o’er life’s solemn main,” and man is linked
to woman as bow is joined to stern—he going
first, she following after. True; sometimes a
vessel moves stern first; but, as a general rule,
man is in advance. The helm is at the stern;
and the helm it is which steadies and controls.
So does conservative woman control and steady
veering, plunging man. But the helmsman is
never captain of the ship, and man remains always
the officer on the bridge.—Yours, A.G.
• * •
The Adelaide divorce-suit, Wear v. Wear
and Pophani, the latter a prominent Gawler (S.A.)
medico, about which society has been whispering
for some time, was almost on all-fours with a
recent big London case, and resulted in favour of
Dr. Popham, who was accused by Wear of playing
Adonis with his wife, a fetching Venus of middle
age. The defence was that Mrs. Wear suffered
from epilepsy, and was, therefore, unreliable on
oath. The evidence was unfit for publication, and
the daily papers, therefore, duly published it in
full, with 13 headings and much snuffle.
The big London case aforesaid had one interest-
ing feature of its own. It transpired that the
doctor was in the habit of calling his lady patient
“Dearie,” and when he was asked whether that
was an ordinary practice on the part of medicoes,
he said he believed it was.
The worst feature in the Adelaide case, com-
mented upon by the Judge, was the bringing of a
little girl, not eight years of age, as a witness to
her mother’s alleged dishonour. The jury was
asked to believe that this little child remembered
recognising the doctor’s voice when he visited her
mother years before, when the witness was
only four years old !

• • •
When John Downer, Q. 0., went to Eng-
land, some years ago, as S.A. representative to
the Imperial Conference, it was cabled that at the
inaugural meeting he read his speech, because
otherwise he felt that he could not retain suffi-
cent command over his emotions to refrain from
tears. Latterly, the Opposition have been
criticising Playford, tin newly-appointed Agent-
General, and among other things affirmed that he
is not adapted to carry out the ornamental part of
the duties. In reply, Kingston said : “We don’t
want a man with the tone and style of a courtier.
We don’t want a gentleman (‘And you haven’t
got one,’ chipped in Ishmael Grainger) who, every
time he totters with trembling knees into the
august presence of the Secretary of State, has to
put his words in writing lest the solemnity of the
occasion should bring on an irresistible fit of
weeping.” It was noticed that everybody in the
Assembly, except John Downer, laughed just
here.

Dear Bulletin, —It’s only due to “T.G.’s ”
graceful reflection upon iny personal cleanliness to
suggest that my skin probably wants washing less
than the brains of the sage who couldn’t (wouldn’t!)
see that I used “rectilinear ” in an approximative

masculine figure, as compared with the feminine,
were so thoroughly recognised by the classic

gallery will show—was frequently accentuated to

Dear Bulletin, — You are satirical upon
the Melbourne parson who asked, “ Why not have
billiard-tables in the house, where the fathers and
sons can play without the evil associations that
usually accompany the game ?” Well, why not?
Billiards is about the healthiest intellectual
pastime on earth if played by daylight, or even by
gaslight, without the accompaniment of undue
liquor. Good for body and mind. A man who
can play billiards at home is a fool if he prefers to
patronise a public table, apart from the “evil
associations” question. Never mind the associa-
tions—their evilness or otherwise is not to the point
here. I was merely about to say that a billiard-
table in the house has one distinct objection.
Too many “ardent lovers of the game” are
for ever coming up to the house in
search of 100-up gratis. If that Melbourne
parson wishes to preserve a mob of fellow creatures
from the “evil associations” of a pub. billiard-
room, let him by all means have a table in his
home. They’ll come in dozens and bring their
friends with them. They will start by suggesting
one night a week for a pleasant billiard gathering,
and by degrees they will work up to six nights a
week. They will come on Sundays if the parson
isn’t there to stop them. A billiard-table in tin-
home is well-nigh impossible for a good-tempered
father with a large circle of acquaintances. After
a month or two he hasn’t a home left—only a
house and a billiard-table.—A Home Player.
Cue, the capital of the Murchison, is
rapidly becoming civilised. It has now a sort of
gaol, and the drunks are no longer chained to a
log.
* • •
An English court has decided that a cook
can’t be dismissed without notice for smoking in
the kitchen, though the mistress testified that she
was in the habit of inverting the bowl of her pipe
so that the ashes sometimes dropped into the
breakfast. This settles one question anyhow ;
woman has a right to smoke if she wants to. The
burning question now is—does the law recognise
her right to chew tobacco ?
• • •
The Queen, a London society paper, asks,
“Are proposals premeditated ? ” It wants to
know whether the average society male gives him-
self away to a girl after serious consideration, or
whether he generally does it on the wild unpre-
meditated hop because he is charmed by the sight
of a pair of white shoulders and a large expanse
of back, and is excited with champagne, and his
head is going round with the giddy waltz. The
balance of probability is that some proposals are
unpremeditated, and are mainly owing to a great
unsurveyed allotment of skin on the upper part of
the lady, and that some, especially in the case of
girls with freckles, are done after serious and pro-
found thought. A much more interesting ques-
tion is—which suitor feels himself a bigger and
more complete ass after the event ? Probably
both of them do.

Brown Sugar
(For the Bullerin.)
She dwells beyond Goodooga,
A daughter of old Mike,
Jim names her “ Sweet Brown Sugar
For reasons lover-like.
For among Australee misses
He hasn’t chanced to meet
Any damosel whose kisses
Are half so sugar-sweet.
And no other girl possesses—
To the west of Sydney town—
Such coils of shiny tresses
Which—like her eyes—are brown
Very fervently he wishes
(For her sake more than his)
That his share of “ loaves and fishes ’
Were larger than it is.
ja or vacuous are his coffers—
Alack ! they’ve been so long—
Yet, minstrel-wise, he offers
His lady-love a song.
And, some poet’s phrase to borrow,
He would thirstily drink up—
To the lees—his “ cup of sorrow ”
Were “ Brown Sugar ” in that cup.
Ah ! her eyes are brown and tender !
And, when they light on Jim,
Make him wish the Lord would send her
To sweeten life for him.
W arrego.
THE BREAKER.
“PSYCHICAL RESEARCH."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The marble, pearly white and cold,
Seems glowing pink and warm,
As Cupid's amorous arms enfold
That palpitating form.
With fellow feeling we regard
The passionate embrace.
Till Maude leans o’er to read the card
That rests upon the base.
“ Why I—Cupid—Psyche—that’s the same !’’
Her twinkling eyes flash bright.
** So now I understand their game
In turning out the light,”
And, nestling close her golden head,
Quite heedless of propriety,
“ I think we’d better join,” she said,
“That Psychical Society."
Melb.

Mr. and Mrs. Solomon Meyer, of Goul-
burn (N.S.W-). last week celebrated their golden
wedding. They were married at Launceston,
V.D.L., in 1844. Mr. Meyer’s brother, Abraham,
with his wife and seven children, were lost in the
Dunbar, at Sydney Heads, in 1857.

In Maoriland it has been ruled that an
auctioneer’s “dealingtalk” amounts to a guarantee.
Why not also business advertisements, political
pledges, and newspaper puffs ? Tall talk from an
auctioneer is no worse than the falsehood of other
professions.

Dear Bulletin,—lie “ Sappho Smith’s ”
remarks about a parasol in the hands of a woman-
fencer —many a man has gone under for want of
knowing what can be done with a stick too light
for knocking-down purposes. The straight thrust
of a cane in the stomach will floor a man as
effectually as a bludgeon-blow on the head.
Delivered in the neck, it is half-murderous, and
in the eye, entirely so,—A.

The bard Fitz Gibbon, of Melbourne,
cabled his personal congratulations to the Duchess
of York on the birth of her son, and to Mine.
Carnot on the loss of her husband, the murdered
President. Other people communicated through
the Governor or the French Consul, but the son
of Gibbon objects to being lost among the ruck of
sympathisers. It is even rumoured that he sent
to each lady a little poem which he had dashed
off. If he didn’t, it is the first time F. Gibbon
missed a chance to inflict a poem on somebody.

Pastor Bevan and a few other pushing
Melb. dispensers of grace have been giving “ mid-
day services for business men.” The congregations
consist almost entirely of raw-boned, elderly
females, and frosty-nosed, modcrately-young
females, and a sprinkling of fat, wheezy, vener-
able, well-to-do unemployed, to whom church-
going is a disease. The Melbourne business man
knows that if he made a point of going to church
in business hours, or even in lunch-hour, it would
presently mean a meeting of creditors.

One school of scientists asserts that the
lives of hospital nurses are greatly curtailed by
their occupation.
A healthy girl of 17, devoting herself to hospital nurs-
ing, dies, on an average, 21 years sooner than a girl of
the same age moving among the general population. A
hospital nurse, at the age of 25, has the same expectation
of life as a person of the age of 58 in the ordinary com-
munity.
Will the life-insurance actuaries and capable
physiologists endorse this gloomy view of a pro-
fession which is becoming a craze amongst even
society girls? If so, the contest for nursing
vacancies will resolve itself into a struggle between

PAT: “Spake to the droiver; I’m the horse.”
Lurking Death and Feminine Desire for For-
bidden Knowledge! Speaking bluntly, the spur
that first animates nursing volunteers is not
philanthropy, not womanly kindness, not a thirst
for noble deeds, nor even the mere desire to make
an independent living, but a craving for that sort
of knowledge which a mistaken social system
hides from maidenhood under hermetical seals.
Hence the vulgar and universal, if brutal, belief :
“ Wot a trained nuss dunno, ain’t wuth knowin’.”

Sydney owns a natty diddler and a fool
publican. Jeremy’s game was to present himself
to Bung, show him a bit of greasy paper declared
to be his will, wherein he bequeathed to mine host
all his property and several thousands in cash.
After which, Diddler would borrow a trifle of
silver. Alas ! alas ! it only panned out, in three
prospects, 11s. fid. in cash for a contra of seven
days in the cage for fraud. Gross injustice ! The
man deserving punishment was Bung.

A suggestion to forward a congratulatory
cable to the Duke of York on the birth of his son,
was laughed out of Adelaide City Council. J.
Zimri Sellar threatened, if the Mayor took the
proposal in earnest, to move an amendment and
talk for hours on it, but his jaws were spared.

“IMITATION
is the
SINCEREST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
BECAUSE they have the best reputation ana
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their
fame and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
bare-faced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet, in face of all they seem to thrive,
and the paper who hounds them down most
thrives fattest on their advertisements and cracks
champagne over each balance-sheet. Clements
Tonic and Fletcher’s Fills have never yet stooped
to_ deception and misrepresentation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as
to their value can be found in every district, and
such being the case it is unnecessary to further
extol their virtues.

Napoleon Bonaparte I,
EMPEROR OF THE FRENCH.
THE SECRET OF HIS SUCCESS.
How he Preserved his Nervous System.
NAPOLEON BONAPARTE, second son of
Charles Bonaparte and his wife, Letizia de
Ratnolins, was born at Ajaccio, in Corsica, on the
15th of August, 1769. In the year 1779 he entered
the Royal Military School of Brienne le Chateau.
There he remained till the autumn of 1784, when
he was transferred to the Military School of Paris,
according to the usual routine. He lost his father
in 1785, and the same year he was commissioned
a second lieutenant of artillery, and served as such
at Valence and other garrisons.
Napoleon attempted to obtain military power
in Corsica, but having failed, he fled to France,
where his career as a soldier and leader of men
became the most wonderful known to history, both
before and after that period. He was soon pro-
moted to chef de batallion, and commanded the
artillery at the siege of Toulon, where he highly
distinguished himself, and was the author of the
plan of attack which led to the fall of the place.
He was then promoted general of brigade.
On the fall of Robespierre, Napoleon was ordered
to take command of an infantry brigade in the
Army of the West. Thinking this beneath his
dignity, he refused to comply with the order, and
was discharged as a general. He was, however,
appointed second in command of the Army of the
Interior, Oct. 5, 1795, under Barras, and repelled
next day an attack of the sections of Paris.
Napoleon was appointed to the command of the
Army of Italy, Feb. 23, 1796; he married on
March 9, same year, Josephine Tascher de la
Pagorie, widow of General Victome Alexandra
de Beauharnais, and left Paris immediately for
Italy. Napoleon’s great military genius flashed
forth in its full brilliancy in this his first
campaign. His power lay in the rapidity and
boldness of his decisions, and in the untiring
energy with which he carried them out, confound-
ing his enemies by the suddenness and lightning
rapidity of his blows, which never gave them time
to recover. He performed a very clever move, by
penetrating the point of junction of two strong
armies which were opposing the French army, the
one consisting of 20,000 Piedmontese under Colli,
the other of 38,000 Austrians, under Beaulieu, and
succeeded most wonderfully, although the French
army was only 36,000 strong.
From now his career was one continuation of
triumphal successes, until almost the whole of
Europe and other important parts of the world
was at his mercy. He soon became elected First
Consul for life, and in 1804 he assumed the title of
Emperor, and this was decreed by the Senate
shortly afterwards. The institutions which he
created, and which still remain, were the restored
church, the judicial system, the codes, the system
of Local Government, the University, the Bank
of France and the Legion of Honour. Space will
not permit to describe his unfortunate end, but
suffice it to say, that the wonderful nerve-power,
which he possessed and preserved to the last, was
absolutely due to a secret medical preparation
discovered in Corsica, by a medical relation of his,
and without which his nerves would have been
shattered long before he had reached his great
triumphs.
This great nerve invigorator is known as the
Napoleon Vigour Powder, and is now being intro-
duced into Australia, the sole Australian agents
being The Imperial Drug Company, 239 Eliza-
beth-street (near Bathurst-street), Sydney, who
will forward free pamphlets and all particulars on
application.
The Napoleon Vigor Powder is simply marvel-
lous for restoring a broken-down nervous system,
either brought on through early follies or other-
wise, and an excellent preventative against and
cure for nervousness, despondency, melancholia,
lost vitality and debility. The remedy is sold in
boxes at 65., or in courses of 5 boxes at 255. Double
course, 455. posted. Pamphlets, remedies, &c.,
and all particulars sent strictly unobserved by the
agents, the Imperial Drug Co., 239 Elizabeth-street,
Sydney, to whom all cheques, Post Office money-
orders, postal notes, &c., should be made payable.

A prominent official in Brisbane Lands
Office lately brought to light certain irregularities
on the part of a northern brother official, who was
<li-<missed. Within a month or two the Brisbane
official was arrested (on information of a third
official) on a charge of embezzling £325. Set an
official to catch an official.
In the Adelaide police force it takes a
constable about 20 years to become a lance-corporal
if is he lucky, and 35 years to get the three stripes
of a sergeant. Field, appointed sub-inspector the
other day, has been more than 40 years in the
service.
Gus. Sternberg suicided at Avenel (Vic.),
the other day; Having first cut his throat, he
loaded his gun, and discharged both barrels into
his body. Still finding himself alive and com-
paratively well, he loaded up again and fired once
more. Then he died. Poor wretch ! If he found
it as hard to live as to die, he must have had a
rough time.
Dear Bulletin, —Four people at Inverell
were responsible for 8 out of 23 successful guesses
in your last Missing Word competition. Inverell
must be a darned lucky place to live in. Could
you oblige a Constant Reader with figures, showing
the percentage of correct guesses produced by this
not very large town, and particulars of the
natural features of the district ? I think
I have noticed, too, that Young is a
good town for guessing in. How do you
account for these things ?—Constant Reader.
[The Bulletin doesn’t profess to be able to fully
account for those things. Perhaps, however, the
lucky Mr. MTlveen invested in many guesses on
the strength of his first big win of £l5O. Possi-
bly some light is thrown on the Inverell wins by
the following note, received, last week, from Mr.
MTlveen aforesaid, who, it would appear from
his letter-heading, is a “ share-broker, stock and
station and mining agent, and auctioneer,” at
Inverell]: —
Editor Bulletin, —Am glad to see that I have again
been successful. Kindly hand the odd lie. 3d. to the
