“ Titus Salt ” in reply : ,
Dear Bulletin,— l will endeavour to make my answer
to Mr. Collins as brief as possible. Mr. Collins’ theory
is that the “ moral tone" nation is the most durable and
the best fighting material; mine is that the crowd which
has a moral tone, and the crowd which hasn’t one, take
an equal chance, and come out much the same in the end.
As regards his particular instances: (1) There is no evi-
dence that the Macedonians were a moral race ; it was
the Macedonian phalanx, not the Macedonian morals,
which swamped Sparta. Also, the generalship of Philip l
had something to do with it. (2) Dunbar wat a generals’,
not a soldiers’, battle. So was Naseby. So were most'
other battles. The Ironsides carried all before them, not
because of their morality, but because of the splendid
generalship of Cromwell. When their moral descendants,
under the moral William 111., met the dissolute
Household troops of Louis XIV. under Luxembourg, who
was a brilliant general and a rake of the worst order, they
went to unutterable smash. When they were led by
Marlborough, who was the greatest leader and about the
worst villain of his time, they were victorious. When
their descendants, again, under the Duke of York, ran up
against the descendants of the men whom Marlborough
conquered, led by such generals as Pichbqru, they went
under. The generals, not the morals, were responsible
every time. (2) Tom Collins asks, re the Janizaries,
“ VVher is dot barty now ?’’ Well, the Janizaries are just
where the Ironsides and the Huguenots and the Pilgrim
Fathers are—dead. But the last of them died fighting
grimly against stupendous odds, and, as far as pluck was
concerned, they never degenerated anyhow. (3) Brother
Collins asks me to compare Massachusetts with Mexico
and see whether the moral or the immoral lot—the sons of
the Pilgrims or the sons of the riff-raff who followed Cortez
—will survive. 1 have compared them, and the result is
an open question. As far as can be estimated from the
available figures—the Pilgrims, if left to themselves,
would have about died out. The tendency to die out
among their descendants is very marked, and if it were
not for the constant stream of foreign immigration it
would probably be very hard to find even the semblance
of an alleged Pilgrim now. Even as it is, New England,
the land of the Pilgrim Fathers, is fast degenerating into
a wilderness of deserted farms and abandoned agricul-
tural lands, and its condition is one of the apparently
unsolvable problems of the Union. At all events, the
present Mexicans are a happier race, and have evolved a
vastly less corrupt system of Government, than the
alleged sons of the Pilgrims, and as to their great men,
Massachusetts never produced so great a man as Juarez,
and 1 don’t think it produced any greater than Miguel
Hidalgo or Porkirio Diaz. If Tom Collins estimates the
Mexican from the typical “greaser” in the American
western drama, or from American border literature, or
from American ideas generally, he is very far astray. (4)
Even granting that Sparta was all that Tom Collins
claims-which it wasn’t—what was its fighting record
compared to that of the Six Nations whose handful of
warriors dominated all the country from the St. Lawrence
to Virginia for at least two centuries? And if they were
moral, the fact has somehow been left out of history.
Also, the Greek, who survived 400 years of foreign
tyranny, was not the moral Spartan, but the hideously
immoral wreckage of the Lower Empire. lam perfectly
willing to admit that the moral men and the moral races
of this world have left a splendid record behind them.
But the immoral races and the immoral men have done
likewise, and the results, so far as I can see, are about
equal.
Dear Bulletin, — Be the Piddington inci-
dent. Despite Dr. M'Laurin’s disclaimer as one
who knew of no undue influence, the business
looks and smells fishy. Piddington might have
been a bona-fide civil servant, and the Sydney
University Senate might have been quite right in
holding that their paid servants should not sit in
Parliament; but they should serve all with the
same sauce. One University professor is a member
of N.S.W. Leg. Council, and two of the lecturers
sat in the late Assembly. The seat of one of these
latter was actually challenged on the ground that
he was a paid public servant, but it was held that
he was not the holder of “ an office of profit under
the Crown.” How does this square with Pickling -
ton’s case ?—Y,
Stead’s book about Chicago is dull stuff,
after all —boom et prceterea precious little. Stead
has been a bright writer in his time ; but it looks
as if he had written himself out. His Review,
which at first was readable, has grown into a
dreary magazine of clippings, and the introduc-
tory articles are heavy as lead. There is a Non-
conformist blight on it. Luckily, Stead has earned
name and fame as the boss exploiter of the Non-
conformist conscience, and is under no risk of
ending his days in a poor-house. What is chiefly
evident in “ If Christead came to Chicago ” is the
height and depth and breadth and thickness of
Stead’s knowledge of the Scarlet Woman. The
book is a splendid advertisement for Chicago
bagnios. A scarlet plan is prefaced, which
tells strangers exactly where they are to go,
and interesting descriptions of the “ best houses ”
follow, with names and all necessary details. The
Scarlet Woman is introduced at the beginning of
the book, and Stead is continually harking back to
the congenial subject. Sketches of municipal cor-
ruption flit casually across the foreground, but
She of Babylon remains always behind, vast and
magnificent. Off the Scarlet Woman, Stead does
not do himself justice. As the Chicago press
said, there is nothing new in the book, though the
exposure of boodling is novel. But there is one
thing abundantly clear, which is that Stead had a
devil of a good time in Chicago.
The half-yearly div. of the IS.Z. Insurance
Co., at the rate of 12\ per cent, per an., is now pay-
able. Last year the available balance, after
paying all losses and defraying all expenses, was
.£71,703 10s. 5d., out of which the shareholders
received £27,500 in divs. and bonus. Recognising
the possible depreciation of investments, the
directors wisely allotted £lO,OOO to investment
fluctuation account, and £15,000 for depreciation
of investments, which left a balance of £19,203
10s. sd. to be carried forward. These figures
speak volumes in favour of the management of the
company, and the shareholders are to be con-
gratulated for having invested their funds in a
company whose business is conducted on so sound
and careful a basis. The New Zealand Insurance
Company is the oldest and largest company of
the kind in New Zealand, and from its commence-
ment has been deservedly popular in that colony.
The market price of its shares is 50 per cent, above
par. The head office of the company is at Auck-
land. Mr. Warwick Weston, general manager.
It has branches and agencies in all the colonies.
In Loudon, Glasgow, India, China, Japan, Cape
of Good Hope, and San Francisco, and for over
20 years its business in New South Wales has
been under the management of Mr. Hague Smith,
in the company’s freehold premises No. 81 Pitt-
street, Sydney.*
M’Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
We are pleased to be able to state that good news from
London still continues to arrive, the recent rise in wool
being fully maintained.
At our local auctions this week the demand was strong
and sales readily effected. In addition to the usual odd
lots of greasy, and country and local scoured, a few clips
of new wool passed under the hammer, and for these bid-
ding was very animated, with a consequence that they
realised more money than when disposed of hers last
year. This gives good promise for the approaching season,
and we must again state that everything seems to point
to a slight increase in prices for the clip of 1894-6.
The shearing trouble seems to have ended in smoke,
and we must congratulate shearers upon their good sense
in keeping out of the clutches of long-winded agitators.
It is now almost a certainty that our market in the
coming season will he patronised by American buyers,
and with wool on the free list and the Yankee presence in
oursale-Doom, must give a big stimulus to business.
Sydney, July 16, 1894.

Proclamation by His Excellency the Right
Honorable Sir Robert William Duff, a member of
Her Majesty’s Moat Honorable Privy Council,
a Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished
Order of Saint Michael and Saint George,
Governor and Oommander-in-Chief of the Colony
of New South Wales and its Dependencies.
(1.5.) By Deputation from His Excellency :
Fbkdk. M. Darlky, Lieutenant-Governor.
Whereas the Commissioners of Fisheries for New
South Wales have reported that the natural
supply of fish in George’s River has become ex-
hausted to such a degree as to require rest for the
recovery thereof: Now, therefore, I, Sir Robert
William Duff, the Governor of the Colony of New
South Wales, in pursuance of the provisions of
the Fisheries Act, 1881, and with the advice of the
Executive Council, do, by this my Proclamation,
declare that the several portions of that water
specified in the Schedule hereto appended shall be
closed against the use of fishing-nets for a period
of two years from the date hereof.
Given binder ray Hand and Seal, at Government
House, Sydney, this sixth day of July, in
the year of our Lord one thousand eight
hundred and ninety-four, and in the fifty-
eighth year of Her Majesty’s Reign.
By His Excellency’s Command,
GEORGE R. DIBBS.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.B.C. Grand Nat. Steeplechase was
another sermon on the vanity of early fancies for a
jumping handicap, in which weight may mean more or
less, just according to the state of the weather. This
time the ground, as it happened, was all in favour of the
least-known performers that had the least to carry, and a
9st. 3lbs. winner was followed home by four other bottom
weights. Four favourites, including the “ pot,” Coranga,-
mite, fell during the races, and two more of the fancied
ones—Busaco and Medicine—came in lame. The greatest
disappointment was Actress, the handsome S.A. mare,
who looked in good condition, but appeared quite unable
to gallop through the mud.
Glenview, the despised outsider of them all,
managed to get round the course. Miller, junr., of the
show-printing firm, had a commission to back Glenview
for a lady. She had dreamed it and she wanted five
shillings on. So the commissioner went around the
paddock, rejecting miserable offers of 25 to 1 (the highest
price some of them were laying about anything), until he
found a broad-minded Hebrew who was_ prepared to do
the handsome thing. “ Glenview 1” said the reckless
metallician, “give us your half-sovereign, take this ticket
in your left hand, and write down your own price. God
bless you!” So Miller modestly wrote down the wager at
fifties, although he might as well have laid himself a
quarter of a million sterling to the five bob.
♦ ♦ * *
*The “books” laid preposterously short prices
throughout the Plemington O.N. meeting. Even the
published odds, which, in nearly all cases, are more than
a punter can obtain about his fancy, showed the folly of
investing a pound with Ikey Mo for the alleged benefit
(t ride Frank Madden) of a small ring of horse-owners.
On Thursday, before the Steeplechase, the newspaper
odds placed Ikey on velvet after laying the first 15 horses
on the list. The odds to £5OO against each of the fifteen
would leave him a sure winner of from £25 to £9O by the
success of any one of them, whilst the_ remaining nine
horses quoted could be running for a “ skinner.”
• ♦ • *
Mat Dawson, the trainer of Ladas, has been 54
years in the business. He and Mrs. Mat are a “ golden-
wedding” couple, the result of early hours throughout a
breezy bracing life. No fashionable Australian trainer
can hope to last like the Dawsons, Days, and Scolls until
the local whisky improves in quality. These old horsey
identities are good judges of their own blood stock (which
is food and liquor) as well as the equine article.
* * * •
The Bulletin surmised truly that the scratch-
ing of Ladas for Ascot engagements had_ nothing to do
with the shrieks of chapel piety. Speaking at the Eton
celebration on June 4th old-boy Eosebery said : “Never
did I expect to hear I he Provost of Eton congratulate one
of his past pupils on his prospects of winning the Derby.
This is a benediction which should reach far and bring
luck, and lam bound to say-I take this public oppor-
tunity of saying it in answer to representations that have
reached me from various sources, one of them only yester-
day from the secretary of the Anti-Gambling Society—
that I feel no vestige of shame in possessing a good
horse.”
One of the biggest winners on Ladas Derby
was Lord Rosebery’s youthful daughter. Fry, the
leviathan British bookie, was her victim. When he paid
over the £9OOO to Miss Primrose’s commissioner he re-
marked, “ I must have been a myg to lay a big bet like
that (to a bloomin' infant, even) who’s a cross between a
Scotchman and a Jew.”

The pen was put through all Mr. W. A. Dong’s
entries (or Warwick Farm last Saturday, and Rosehill
next Saturday, owing to the death of his mother. Mr.
Long’s sporting proclivities come from the deceased lady s
side of the house. Old “Bill” had a horror of horse-
racing and it was not until his father s death that young
“ Bill ” went on the turf. Mr. Long’s uncle, the father,
by the way. of Mr. W. B. Walford, treasurer of the
A J 0., was the best amateur boxer in Tasmania—in fact,
it was pretty well conceded that there was not a man in
the island, amateur or professional, able to beat him, with
or without the mittens.
* * * *
It is nob often a horse wins a treble on the one,
day, as Aloe did at Grafton by annexing the Grafton Han-
dicap, C.R.J. Club Handicap, and the Special Purse on
the opening day of the Clarence River Jockey Club meet-
ing. Aloe is by Anteros from Sunbeam.
. * * •
The Victorian Governor’s eldest son followed
the hounds for the first time last week, when the “ Mel-
bourne,” under George Watson, scoured the Wernbee.
The nine-year-old hunter rode on to the death, his only
disposition to swerve being from the groom and bearing
rein with which he had been discreetly provided. Hif
mother urged his release; his father thought it wiser fo»
the groom to hold on. As a set-off the youthful sport
was allowed to carry home fch© head of thedeerin triumph
at his saddle-bow. Athletics are coming out strongly ir
this scion of the house of Hopetoun ; haying no playmate!
of his own he may sometimes be seen kicking a football
with one of the Force hanging about the front of Govt.
House with the belated air of not knowing what he 11
there for.
* * • ♦
Melb. Democratic Club is now holding amateur
boxing competitions every Monday night. So far, these
(gratis) exhibitions have been as clever as those for which
the gilded youth of Melb. used to part guineas a couple
of years ago.
* * * *
At a recent Berlin smoking contest, the winner
smoked 10 cigars one after the other within 2 hours. The
second man consumed 7J cigars.
* * * •
At a Maoriland junior football-match a local
man awkwardly missed aim, and, hitting the ball with the
side of his foot, skied it over his head and took his own
side’s goal. The umpire ruled that the goal counted for
the other side. This is regarded as funny by several
critics, but isn’t it merely analogous to a cricket-batsman
knocking down his own wickets when playing a ball too
far back, or playing a ball on to a wicket?
» * • *
A strong tip for the “two Cups” in Melb.
Jewish circles is Isaac. The name may have a deal to do
with it, but the Chosen are backing their opinion.
• * • *
Donald Dinnie is pebbly-beached in Maoriland.
Donald lately has been appearing with a poor variety co.,
and before he does his weight-lifting feats he makes a
very bad speech. He also.plays a jig on the fiddle and
wrestles. Why cannot someone take him in hand?
Surely he is as great a celebrity (in his own line) as
Roberts at billiards or Grace at cricket.
♦ * • •
The most popular feature at a church-spree in
St. Hilda, Melb., one night last week, was a gamble with
toy horses on a kind of spinning-jenny, a game pro-
hibited on all racecourses. Holy associations purify bad
practices. $ ,
Adelaide football. South Adelaide defeated
Norwood, 7 goals to 4 goals. Norwood defeated Poit
Adelaide, 9to 2. Port Adelaide defeated South Adelaide,
7to 2. North Adelaide defeated Port Adelaide, 4to 3.
Norwood defeated North Adelaide, 22 to 1. There is
enough evidence here to disqualify every football-team in
Adelaide.
♦ * * *
Melbournian : “At Croxton I have just been
Studying the bob-bettor and the threepenny. Outside
this cheap racecourse is the mob of fielders, punters,
puntresses, and puntikins, who can’t pay to go in. 1 saw
a little boy running about jingling coppers in his hand,
and a burly bookmaker, in a top coat, said ; ‘’Ere, four
ter one Florry. Yer can’t get better.’ Reminded me of
the ‘ Flowers of the Forest,’ where Kinchin ‘ nimms ’ a
piece of bread and butter from a poor little child.”
* * * * •
Sydney bicyclists Simpson and Kerr had a
good time in Brisbane. Simpson beat Kerr by a wheel
for the Five Mile Amateur Championship in 13 rain. 4-sth
sec., the last mile being run in 2 min. 20 2-sth sec., mak-
ing a record for Australasia. Kerr, however, had a mild
revenge in the mile scratch race, when he won from
Simpson and Browne, who ran a dead-heat for second.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Some weeks or months ago a well-known Syd-
neyite, supposed to be wealthy, lost, late at night, and
whilst more or less excited, £lOOO in billiard-wagers at
a certain club. A dispute arose as to payment of the
njoney—and the club committee decided that he would
have to pay. The sportsman to whom the money was
lost refused to compound for an amount which the loser
offered. “ Very well,” said the latter, “If you don’t take
that you may get nothing. lam about to undergo an
operation which may kill me. Take it or leave it." The
winner left it, thinking—smart man!—that the operation
story was “ all bluff.” The operation was performed and
the loser is dead. And Death pays all gambling-debts.
* • ♦ •
The writer is prepared for a howl from Griffo’s
admirers in Australia when he states that if Griffo and
Jerry Marshall should meet in the States, he would not be
surprised to hear of-the clever little darkie coming out on
top.
l * * ♦ *
lb is surely time the Australian public palled on
the Jim Burge slaughters. They are the essence of
brutality. There is no pretence of a contest, the old
warrior simply undertaking to take the place of a punch-
ing bag for 79 minutes. Last week in Melbourne a deaf
mute smashed him about in a terrible manner for the 20
rounds, but as Burge could stagger to his feet at the con-
clusion it was called a “ draw.” Friday O’Neill is now
billed to smash up Burge—and his hands—for 20 rounds.
A Black Country shin-kicking match would relieve the
monotony.
« * * *
Tim Hegarty, the clever Melb. featherweight,
won the championship of that class last Saturday night
by defeating Jack M'Gowan, on points. Hegarty, whose
peculiar tight with Willis a week or two ago will be
remembered, is a lightning boxer, though he hits less
hard than some men of his weight. In a large ring he
should have no difficulty in keeping his present position,
as he is a quiet boy and not given to “ Griffo ” outbursts.
Good Australian pugilists goto America—before
they die. Tut Ryan, whom Mick Dooley defeated recently
for the championship, is the latest scrapper with trans-
pacific intentions. Ryan, unlike many of his confreres,
is a teetotaller and non-smoker, and should be successful
in Yankeeland.

The Calif/raph Writing Machine,
v-
THE CaLIGRAFH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that th* platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that yonr correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

. .j® light-weight championship remains nn-
decided by the draw which lately resulted between
Dummy Mace and “Ironbark” Burge at Melb.
Both men are_short, thick-set, nuggety scrappers, and
noted for their gluttonous capacity for punishment,
so a big crowd rolled up in anticipation of a blood-and-
nair contest. The fight was, however, a comparatively
tame show, and, taking the whole 20 rounds into con-
sideration, a draw was about the fairest ending.

Dear Bulletin,— The 1.0.G.T. claims to
be on the warpath, and intends an invasion of the
electioneering stump. It lias held a boss-meeting
of lodge officers styled “electoral deputies.” The
duties of these deputies are, generally, to see that
all Good Templars are enrolled and have their
votes secure, and then to try and enthuse them
into voting each for the “ right man ” in his elec-
toral district. My experience of Good Templars,
as voters and I have held office in the Order —is
that their block vote is worth—just nil. Given a
Good Templar who is a conscientious Freetrader,
and he will sooner vote for a beer-guzzling Free-
trader than a water-soaked Protectionist ; and
vice versa. Few, indeed, are the Good Templars
with whom several political issues do not over-
shadow the mere question of teetotal ethics. Many
are theG.T. s who merely join lodges because they
are canny in money matters and yet hanker after
cheap amusement and feminine society. The
lodge-rooms are splendid spooning-places. I
speak by the card. Not 5, not 2 per cent, of the
Good Templars, taking them all round—the luke-
warm, the expediency-mongers, and the earnest
cranks—-really thrill with the idea that they are
atoms in a moral, elevating and regenerating
mission to purify society and to put down a curse.
I know them. They love music-halls songs better
than temperance ditties, and politics of a general
kind touch them more deeply than the one-stringed
temperance fiddle. So, at election times, the votes
of the various sections neutralise each other. As
a political force —until female suffrage obtains—
they don’t count.—Yours,
DIPSO.

A small boy, who died of apoplexy at a
school picnic, was inquested recently in London.
Cause of death : thirty oranges, one cocoa-nut,
11b. of rich cake, and a mince pie, accompanied by
tea, cider, lemonade, “ mineral water champagne,”
and “ hop beer.” Death from stuffocation.

A New Litany.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
From kings and queens and kaisers,
From millionaires and misers,
From unctuous priests who levy tolls
On roads to Heaven and whitewashed souls,
From all the knights of iron jaw,
Who live by jugglery and law,
Good Lord deliver us !
From all the clan of canting,
Who live by wildly ranting;
From all who preach a bigot’s bell
Can ope the gates of Heaven and Hell;
From all who pray with bended pate
And drop—three ha’pence in the plate,
Good Lord deliver us !
From Fat Men and their mandates,
From banks and booms and bandits ;
From all who psalm and song uplift
To sing thy saving virtue—Thrift I
From all the lords of land and rent,
From all the tribe of cent-per-cent,
Good Lord deliver us 1
From every caitiff toiler
Who cringes to the spoiler;
From all the cursed, wolfish clan
Who batten on their fellow-man ;
From every knave who wields a rod
And every slave who hails him God-
Good Lord deliver us 1
Q-

The biggest vine in Victoria is in the
vineyard of L. Giizzi, near Huntley. It trellises
over 35 sq. yds., and, last year, gave 2| tons of
grapes, which yielded 6 hhds. of wine.
A Monaro correspondent:—“ A new man-
ager has arisen to follow ‘ Old Man Mac,’ of Dele-
gate (N.S. W.) Station, This truly grand old man,
known and loved all over Monaro as ‘Old Man
Mac,’ was an identity of over 50 years’ standing as
manager of the station. He was like a father to
the district. Many a man has Mac. gratuitously
set on his feet in this region in days when doctors
were unheard of here. Old Alexander M‘Keachie
was regretted by all.
Editor Bulletin’, —When a man speaks of
patting 8 bags of flour—l6ool bs.—on a camel, and
says the beast got up with the load, it’s about time
the brake was applied. Remember that a camel’s
legs are never thicker than, and certainly not so
muscular as, those of an ordinary Clydesdale horse.
The maximum loads allowed for pack animals are
—elephant, 11201bs. ; camel, 6401b5. ; horse or
mule, according to size, 200 to 3001b5.; bullock, 160
to 2401b5. These are maximum loads, and any-
body looking at a camel will see that he cannot be
expected to carry more than twice as much as a
horse. No camel is so tall as a large elephant—
10ft. or lift, to the top of the shoulder, the hump
is little else than a mass of fat, while its mus-
cular tissue is little more bulky than that of a
d raugh t-horse. —U NT.
Premier Kingston, who once opposed free
education, gave reasons in S.A. Assembly recently
for supporting the system now it has become law.
“ I hope I shall never have to fall back on so lame
an excuse,” exclaimed Griffiths, the Whip of the
party which seeks to upset the Act. “ Don’t be to
sanguine,” advised the Premier good-temperedly.
Whereupon, with a sneer, quoth Solomon : “Evil
communications corrupt good manners.”’ “ Yes,’
responded Kingston, “he is your colleague.”
“Sol” and “Griff ” jointly represent the Northern
Territory —about the biggest single constituency
in the world.
Drake, the Q. Oppositionist, says that in
England he found that people didn’t know there
was any Q. Opposition. The little ring of
absentees who represent “kawlonial opinion”
spread the good tidings of Boodle wraith so
zealously, that the average person is under
the impression that Boodlewraith is supreme, and
that the Labour-party and the Opposition are
only insignificant sections of the people, who
prefer to worship the same deity in their own way.
Even in Sydney Drake found things the same.
Talking to a friend, he happened to mention the
Q. Opposition. “Why,” said the friend, “I
thought there was no Opposition.” “ Who told
you?” “Oh, Mr. Tozer !”
Apropos of the spread of education. There
is a shepherd out towards the Diamantina to whom
reading and writing are equally strangers. He has a
hut on a lonesome part of the run, but even there
the swagger comes along now and then and
demands provender. Generally the shepherd is
out, and then the gaunt wanderer sometimes
bursts the door in and clears the premises. The
last time this happened the shepherd lost patience,
and after that, when he left the hut, he would put
his victuals in a candle-box and plant it carefully
in a hole out in the paddock. The second day
after he adopted this device a sundowner arrived
and rummaged the premises, then finding nothing
he gave way to fury and wrote a quantity of most
unpublishable Barcoo language on the door
with a burnt stick. The reflections which he
put on that door were beyond the power of
any mere city man to express in language. A few
hours later the shepherd came home, and, after
long and serious consideration of the hierogly-
phics, he decided that they were most probably
an important message from the manager. Accord-
ingly, he took the portal off its hinges of green
liide and carried it five miles to a camp of tank-
sinkers to have it deciphered. Eight riba,ld pick-
and-shovel men bore a hand in reading the
message, amid the obscene merriment of the camp,
and when they were through the shepherd broke
the door into flinders against a tree, bounded on
his hat, and poured forth an objurgation which
made even the tank-sinkers fly for their lives.
He is now learning the alphabet off the brands on
an old bullock-hide, so that he may decipher the
next swagman’s message himself and go in im-
mediate pursuit of the offender.


Parkes has been bombarded with con-
gratulations on the attainment of his 79th birth-
day last Sunday. A Sydney D.T. interviewer
went with a blithesome, springy step to dive into
the old man’s recollections of infancy, and ex-
tracted much information regarding his capacity
for goo-goo utterance and mud-pie manufac-
ture. Singularly enough, however, the veteran
omitted the one thing material, and did not say
that he owes his strength and health, under Pro-
vidence, to a judicious use of Marshall’s Tonic

“ An Unequal Match,” staged on Saturday
at Sydney Criterion, is one of those quaint, old-
world comedies, belonging to an almost forgotten
variety of literature, which used to be called comic
something like 30 years ago. To-day it is hope-
lessly out of date as a comedy, and it is hardly a
drama, and it doesn’t fill the bill as a society play.
Nevertheless, it is an exceedingly pretty love-
story in three acts, and calculated to please any
one who has some semblance of poetry in his soul.
In the first act the author arises and shows how
the British baronet of great possessions loved and
married the beautiful daughter of the blacksmith,
and took her away to let loose her Yorkshire
accent in his ancestral halls. The experiment
pans out only moderately well, for the lady, in
her efforts to fit_ herself for her new station,
smites the piano with an arm of might at 5 a.m.,
and otherwise misconducts herself. Then the
experiment succeeds altogether too well, for the
blacksmith’s daughter becomes a society lady of
so stupendous a type that the baronet is utterly
crushed beneath the weight of her dignity, and
instead of lecturing her for her want of tone, he
finds himself admonished in a frozen fashion for
his own.
• • •
The fact that the baronet ia the sketchy
Mr. Greenaway reconciles the audience consider-
ably tootle fate which descends on his brief and
unconvincing head. It is Greenaway’s misfortune,
somehow, always to seem sketchy and unfinished,
and to look as if he was only printed on one side ;
also, he imparts a vague idea of having been
picked before he was ripe. Miss Hilda Spong,
however, compensates for his little deficiencies.
She is pretty and graceful as the blacksmith’s
daughter, though the Yorkshire accent is a strange
and awful circumstance; and later on she
developes into a personage whom every individual
in the house gets mashed on straight away. The
Bulletin contemplates making some poetry
about Miss Hilda when the election is fairly
over and its boots come back from being
mended. Harry Leston makes a large,
loose, sloppy flunkey, who is all that a flunkey
should be. Miss Fitzmaurioe Gill—the lady with
the ancient Egyptian oast of countenance—is a
moderate success as a female of execrable charac-
ter ; and this paper would like to say something
kind about Mr. Fabert, a highly-intelligent actor
with an aggravating stoniness of expression which
suggests road-metal. Some of Spong’s finest
scenery is put into this production, particularly a
view of one of those small German kingdoms
where an athletic person can stand in the centre
and heave coal into all the adjacent States.
“ Pygmalion and Galatea ” ia announced for next
Saturday, and The Bulletin is haunted by a fear
that Mr. Greenaway intends to be Pygmalion.
** * *
Feeling it necessary fco do something to
demonstrate the perpetual fitness of things, Mr.
Rignold has staged “Uncle Tom’s Cabin” at Syd-
ney Majesty’s, and the frozen Ohio river, over
which Miss Watts-Phillips effects her escape,
makes the wintry audience feel as if the key of the
front door had been mislaid down its back. But
to compensate for this coolness, Uncle Tom is sold
each evening to Albert Marsh, the warmth of
whose language is sufficient compensation for any-
thing. Mr. Marsh is one of the most whole-souled
and satisfactory villains on the Australian stage,
and the wickedness of the drama rests mostly on
his shoulders, the other ruffian having a bad habit
of talking as if his mouth were full of corn, which
seriously diminishes his effectiveness. The un-
satisfactory feature of “ Uncle Tom ” is that, of
the innumerable characters in the drama, the
great majority only flit across the stage and dis-
appear. The two tame dogs, which start after
Miss Watts-Phillips, presently wander into the
wings and vanish. The ruffian with the in-
djstinct utterance is thrown down a preci-
pice, and leaves his brains on the scenery.
Topsy, in the person of Miss Emma Chambers,
breaks things to a song-and-dance accompaniment
for one exciting half-hour, and then retires, and
little Eva is fetched away by the angels a few
minutes before 10. This is a realistic effect, a
patch of Paradise being visible at the back of the
stage, and if George Rignold were seen in the far
distance as an archangel, the effect would be com-
plete. The same lot of angels—one of whom
giggles violently all the time—call for Uncle Tom
about 11, and take that dreadful black elocutionist
up aloft. Hosea Easton’s nigger, if he is memor-
able for anything at all, will be remembered as
the most un-eloquent nigger on record. A lot of
Jubilee singers have been laid on, and there is
enough singing, step-dancing, and general revelry
to make a high-class variety-show. Most of the
17 scenes are works of art, and in a general way
the management has put on the drama with a fixed
resolve to make it a success, or else die and go to
heaven along with little Eva in the attempt.

The Prodigal Daughter,” at Sydney
Lyceum, is on the high tide of success, and the
Duke wins the Grand National Steeplechase every
evening with a regularity which makes backers
long to meet with a horse like him at Randwick.
One notable example of Bland Holt’s realism is
the crowd which gathers at the racecourse to yell
when this noble animal comes in first with Sass
on his back. It can be confidently recommended
as a typical racecourse crowd, for there isn’t one
face in the lurid gathering which shouldn’t get its
owner six months without the option. If this
isn’t a typical racecourse crowd it is difficult to
imagine what is one. This paper still regards Mr.
Cosgrove with a hollow stare of unbelief, and it
isn’t so sure as it might be about Miss Henrietta
Watson’s heavy father, the old English squire ;
but otherwise it regards the show with a favour-
able eye and bestows its blessing upon it.

Frank Emery had a benefit on Tuesday of
last week at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), which
panned out a handsome cheque for the beneficiare.
The show consisted of the tunnel scene in “ After
Dark,” wherein George Rignold drags Scot Inglis
froth beneath the wheels of the untamed paste-
board locomotive; one act of “As You Like it,”
by Miss Hilda Spong and Oo.; the one-aot play,
14 A Clerical Error,” wherein Mr. Emery made an
excellent impression as the drinky butler; sundry
songs by Harry Rickards and others; and the
comedietta “A Happy Medium.” This last was
tackled by an amateur company, of whom Miss
Ina Wild man earned most commendations.
«* * *
The music of Gov. Robinson’s opera,
“Predatoros,” is an improvement on the libretto.
That is about the baldest rehash of bandits, moun-
tain surprises, lovelorn maidens, fat old fathers,
and sentimental mothers that have pranced about
the stage since Gilbert and Sullivan put them
there. “Predatoros ”is miles behind any of them.
The ballads are the nicest things about Gov. W.
Robinson’s part of the business. Of these, there
are about a baker’s dozen, and as they are all
pretty much alike they will be easy enough to
learn.
** * *
Governor Robinson’s opera may seem dull
enough, but the process of manufacture was
supremely comic. The author privileged to colla-
borate with “Sir William ” in this great work is
a Mr. Hart—the same Hart, possibly, who erst-
while served as Melbourne press interpreter for
Johnny Toole’s practical jokes, and who imper-
sonated the bursts of merriment when that funny
old man used to walk into a tailor’s shop to buy a
box of cough-lozenges. Hart having become the
Gov. ’s Jester, a Bureau of Libretti, or some similar
public department, was invented for his benefit by
the Westralian Government, and in his capacity
of Bureaucrat the literary wag brought his brain
to bear upon Robinson’s opera. Meanwhile the
musician wrestled with the same matter in his com-
posing-room at Government House, the Bureau
and the vice-regal den being connected by tele-
phone. As soon as Hart had completed a new
lyrio he rang up his noble patron and breathed
the sparkling lines down the tube, where-
upon a voice from afar spluttered, “beautiful!”
“charming,” or “exceedingly quaintand catchy,”
as the case might be. Presently Hart would be
rung up to listen to the bit of music his Excellency
had dashed off in his latest fit of inspiration. The
strains of Sir William’s piano floated up the tele-
phone spout and flooded the Bureau with melody.
Then Hart in his turn answered, “delicious,”
“enchanting,” or “ bright and spirited,” according
to circumstances. And thus the concoction of the
opera proceeded on a sound mutual admiration
system, _ _ The collaborateurs never quarrelled
or criticised each other’s contributions, like,
Gilbert and Sullivan. The course of telephone
composition was interrupted only by Sir William’s
fracious, “See you to-night, I suppose;” to which
[art invariably replied, “ Your Excellency is too
good.” Lie.
** * *
Melbourne shows are much the same as
last week. At the Alexandra, however, they have
renounced “Ally Sloper” and revived Dan
Barry’s own particular setting of “After Dark,”
which is again famous for the mad career of its
cardboard train. The rolling stock on the Alex-
andra railway line is not content with a simple
roll. It also pitches and tosses in a briny manner,
so that if the orchestra were to strike up “A
Life on the Wave,” and Dan Barry flew to the
rescue on a raft, it would seem as though a drown-
ing Major Chomley had been saved from mutila-
tion by a passing steam-boat. The music-hall scene
now includes a ballet, lightly clad, but fairly
heavy in movement. The ballet damsels, having
concluded their entertainment, drop into easy
attitudes among the audience, and the villain,
who glances suspiciously at these innocent-seeming
young creatures, is assured by his guilty colleague
that they have all been in trouble for something
or other. Which is a nasty knock for stage
morality. _ “ After Dark ” may run for a second
week, but in any case the management has a large
stock of revivals ready to hand.
** * *
“Ma Mie Rosette ” booms without ceas-
ing at the Princess’s. Its last nights have been
announced for some time past, yet the tail-end of
them had not come into sight at time of writing.
The opera season itself is understood to be closing
on the 27th, and, in that case, the pretty story of
what might have happened to Nellie Stewart if
she hadn’t fainted before it came to pass might as
well hold the boards until then. There is no
occasion to withdraw “Ma Mie Rosette” in the
pride of its popularity. Nellie grows nicer than
ever, if that is possible. Also, Florence Young is
a growing matter for admiration, and Tapley
improves with practice.
** » *
“A King of Crime” goes on smiting his
enemies at Melbourne Royal, and the corpse of
the curdled traitor welters in its gore without fail.
Nobody mourns for this departed brother, or
troubles to carry his remains to the morgue,
although he was shouting bottled toast and water
for the populace five minutes before the King
stiffened him. On the stroke of midnight a troupe
of gay masqueraders dance a can-can without any
consideration for the presence of death in their
midst, so it must often occur to the poor traitor
that he died in vain. He might as well have been
not assassinated a little bit, for all the notice that
they bestow on his remains. Charles Holloway,
the King, absorbs all the honour, and glory, and
red light. From first to last his domestic virtues
and criminal excellence hold the gallery en-
thralled. Some of the King’s popularity is re-
flected upon his gentle blind wife, owing to a
cheerful suspicion that he really gouged out her
eyesight before the curtain rose in order to dis-
tract her attention from his burglarious wig. The
next play on the Royal list is “ A Grip of Iron,”
promised for Saturday.

Young Percy Grainger, latest of Austra-
lian composers and pianists, made his first appear-
ance at a recent Melbourne concert. He com-
pleted his Bach numbers with an absence of self-
consciousness as delightful as his mastery over the
music was wonderful. He seemed to be a good
deal more interested in the construction of the
laurel-wreath that his discoverer, Dr. O’Hara,
presented, than in his own performance after it was
over. He hadn’t a note before him, but the music
fell from his fingers as though he made it as he
went along. Little Grainger was born at Brighton
(Vie.); he was 12 the day before the concert,
but might easily pass for nine; he has a
face of seraphic fairness, and taken altogether,
looks like embodied music. His father designed
Princes Bridge and other fine structures in and
about Melbourne, so there may be but one brain*
remove between the architect and the musician,
When the boy is not practising Bach’s fugues, he
is snmlintr u. ' „ ’
kt carving huge weapons of
ancient Saxon and Norman warfare; for his
genius doesn’t stop at music—he might just as
easily oe turned out painter or sculptor. His
trainer—Herr Louis Pabst—wants to take him to
Europe, if the money can be got together. The
father of this young musician is, through bad luck
and hard times, at present seeking a living on a
S. Australian station.

Shadow pantomime again. At a select Melb. concert, a
night or two ago, the prima donna so placed her mirror

The Melbourne theatrical gala for the starving theatri*
cal charities is tagged all round with morals. Youth is>
more than ever, the sine qua non with a gluttonous
public; the stage is as cruel as the cricket field in this
point. Simply horrible are the sufferings, not only of
hangers-on to the stage in Australia, but of recognised
professionals. And the theatrical gala, at the best, is a
poor measure of relief, considering the mouth of hunger
to be filled. Even the successful veteran surrounds him-
self with young men and women ; there is no place for
the middle-aged, let alone the old. Yet they shoal.
The bifurcated skirt is going to cause trouble. Its
latest victim is a Maryborough (Q.) reporter. Some local
musical amateurs formed an 44 amateur operatic oo.,” and
advertised 44 Maritana," in character. A curly-haired
young lady undertook to personate the pagein bifurcated
skirts. The reporter called them knickers, and said in
print that it ought to draw, hinting that the impending
revelations were beauteous. The lady is adored by a
bank-clerk, who so grievously assaulted the scribe that
the latter is laid up for repairs.
The difference between the 44 King of Crime ” as played
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), and the same piece in Mel-
bourne is mainly the difference between Albert Marsh
and Charles Holloway. No one can take Bill Holloway’s
brother seriously as the villain ; in fact, it is difficult to
take him very seriously as anything. This is where he
differs from Bill himself, who was always taken seriously,
not to say lugubriously, even in his most festive moments.
Robert Buchanan, the dramatist, is described as “a
big, bald-headed man, with spectacles, who takes himself
tragically.”
Mr. J. P. Macdonald, for many years business-manager
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), is still, his numerous friends
will regret to learn, very ill, and is an inmate of a private
hospital in Sydney.
Alfred Maltby is returning to Australia as manager of
the London Comedy Co., a joint-stock oonoern, capital
£20,000. He was here some seven years ago, and was
the best feature in a good many of these dreary
mother-in-law- comedies with which Harry St. Maur was
identified. His best character was the fashionable elderly
rake, somewhat inflamed about the nose, very uncertain
on his two wicked old legs, and full of sinful reminiscences
of the ballet, and in this peculiar line of business he had
few superiors.

Mr. John Bennett exhibits an interesting relic of the
old Vic. Theatre, Pitt-street, Sydney, being the pay-
sheets for June 12th and 19th, 1847—just 47 years ago.
Amongst the autographs—3l in number—acknowledging
that the “ ghost walked,” are those of J. Gordon Griffiths,
John and Frank Howson, Andrew Torhing, Frank Nesbitt
M'Cron, Madame Oarandini, Mrs. Thompson (mother of
Mrs. Charles Young, afterwards Mrs. Herman Veein). and
Mrs. Guerin. Only two of the 31 now live—Andrew
Torning and Mrs. Guerin, now Mrs. Richard Stewart,
senr., and mother of Mrs. Harwood, Nellie and Maggie
Stewart, and Richard, jun. The pay-sheet does not dis-
close the salaries paid at that time.

Christie Murray was lately playing a part vice another
man { indisposed, in “A Social Butterfly” at the Opera
Comique. Murray had to act as peacemaker between the

Charles Saunders, the fat and tuneful tenor, was the
only person present at the exhumation of the Needle
bodies, in Melbourne, who had no business to be there.
Queer taste to turn out before 7.30 on a bitter, foggy
morning to see corpses unearthed, Saunders will, no
doubt, soon be singing, with much realism, “Down
among the Dead Men ” and “ The Sexton.”
Charles Turner in his heyday was superb. He always
wanted the nuances of art, but bis vigour was irresistible.
He made much money and spent much, and years of
success left him still at the collar. To his horror, he
grew 'Stout, as tenors will. He never shirked his work,
his voice kept going, going. Now it and he are gone.
The N.S.W. 2nd Infantry Regiment will hold an enter
tainment at Sydney Royal on Thursday, 26th, in aid of
the funds of the recreation-rooms and library. The best
professional dramatic talent locally available has been
secured, and the Sydney Fencing Club is also kind
enough to assist. The dress-circle has already been rushed,
and the trouble will be want of room. “ A Red Rag,”
“Wedlock,” and “A Clerical Error” will form part of
the bill. The object is to furnish what is now a bare room
and get some books and periodicals so that the men may
have a place wherein to meet between parades pleasanter
than a street corner or the usual pub.
“ YOUR LIFE IS NOT WORTH A STRAW.”
Nob worth a straw, eh ? Then it was worth just
nothing—nothing at all. Who has not used that
comparison a thousand times to express absolute
worthlessness? A straw? The wind blows it
away, fire burns it up, cattle tread it in the mud,
it rots by the roadside. What of it? Who cares
for a straw ?
Yet this is exactly what a doctor recently said
to one of his patients, “ Your life is not worth a
straw.” How much is a doctor worth who will
speak so to one that trusts him, and has no hope
bub in his skill? For my part, if he were up for
sale at auction, I would bid one straw tor him—no
more. Even if what he said was true, he had no
right to say it. Such a doctor is more likely to
kill with his tongue than to cure with his drugs.
A woman tells the story, and she tells it well.
If it doesn’t sound like the truth, then I don’t
know what ever does. The dates and the facts
are all there, plain and orderly.
‘‘In the summer of 1878,” she says, “I found
myself feeling tired, languid, low-spirited, and
weak. I felt as if some evil were about to happen.
My appetite was poor, and after eating I had
excruciating pain at my loins and sides. There
was a horrible gnawing pain at the pit of my
stomach, and a rising in the throat as if I should
choke. My head felt as though I had a ton weight
on it. Gradually I got worse, and for months
could take only liquid food. At night I lay awake
for hours altogether.
“Later on I suffered greatly from nervous
prostration. My legs trembled and shook so I
feared to fall. If a knock came to the door I
trembled from head to foot. I had frequent attacks
which began with palpitation of the heart and
sudden stoppage of the breath. At these times I
was speechless and helpless. They say I looked
like a corpse, cold and bloodless, my finger-nails
and lips having turned black. After a while this
would pass off, leaving me weak and prostrate.
I got so emaciated and thin that I was only a bag
of bones, and so weak I had to take hold of the
furniture to steady myself as I crossed the room.
As time went on the nervousness and forebodings
of evil so increased that I feared I should go out
of my mind. The neighbours said it would be a
mercy if the Lord would release me from my
sufferings.
“In this condition I continued for over four
years, during xohich time I consulted jive doctors,
bub nothing they gave me did any good. They all
said my ailment was heart disease, and one said,
* Your life is not worth a straw.'
“ In despair I gave up taking physic, as I felt
that nothing would save me. In May, 1882, ten
years ago, a lady (Mrs. Richardson) called at my
house, told me of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup,
and strongly advised me to try it. I did so, and
felt somewhat better after the first bottle; and by
the time I had taken three bottles I was completely
cured. From that to this I have had no return of
the attacks, and am so strong I can do any kind
of work. Bub for Seigel’s Syrup I should have
been in ray grave long ago. I wish others to know
this, and will answer any who call or write.”
(Signed) Emma Wiokenden (wife of William
Wickenden, gardener), Pembroke Villas, 123,
Moffat Road, Thornton Heath, March 17th, 1892.
So it turned out that her life was not only worth
a straw, but worth a whole golden harvest of health
and better days. Yet no thanks to the doctors.
Her complicated symptoms puzzled and alarmed
them, to be sure, but why ? Is it not the doctors’
duty to understand such things ? Most assuredly.
Just as a lawyer should know the law, or a pilot
the rooks, tides, and lights of a coast. Had some
of these medical men known that Mrs. Wicken-
den’a malady was indigestion and dyspepsia, and
not heart disease, they might possibly have re-
lieved her. But, confused by the symptoms, they
were blind to the cause. We may well wonder if
there are many such doctors in England.
Cases like this show that the clear sight belonged
to Mother Seigel; and to her remedy hosts of
people in this country are indebted for physical
salvation when, in very truth, their lives seemed
as straws.
Remember, this was ten years ago, and th«
malady has not returned, showing that the cure
was a permanent one.

The first type-writer was patented in 1714
by an engineer named Mill, but it was not unto
the last decade that the idea was sufficiently
developed to make it of any practical value. The
necessity of embossing literature for the blind was
the germ of the invention.
A Russian savant has astonished the
scientific world by proving by experiment that
the human body is an electric pile, and that the
skin sends off over the whole surface an endless
number of electric sparks, so small that they can
not be discerned by the naked eye, yet so distinct
in reality that they can be photographed.
A Christian-evidence lecturer, the other
evening, gave an up-to-date discourse, in Christ-
church (M.L.), on “ The Plagues of Egypt.” As
he warmed up to his subject he waxed realistic,
and said that while the plague of boils was afflict-
ing the people, Pharoah “stood up to all his
meals.” But seeing a snigger among the pews he
hastened to explain that the reason he stood up
was that the chairs had all got boils on them.
Dr. Roger S, Chew, of Calcutta, editor of
the Indian Journal of Pharmacy, is the most
systematic leper-curer that The Bulletin has yet
heard from. He publishes a record of 1034 cases
treated, and 422 cured; a cure being effected in
every case (256 in all) in which gynocyanaurizarin
(or C.0.N.) was the agent used. It is worth
noting that Dr. Chew ascribes 148 of his cases
directly to communication of the disease by
vaccination.
The protest by Pastor Miller, of Sydney,
against the action of N.S.W. Parliament in
“omitting to acknowledge God’’at its meetings,
recalls an occasion many years ago in the days when
the President of the Victorian Legislative Council
used to open proceedings with the Lord’s Prayer.
The prayer-book was once mislaid, and the old
gentleman foolhardily started to recite from
memory. He rattled along a bit, and finished it
something like this ; —-“Thy will be dumb; give
us this day our daily bread, and forgive us our
trespasses, as we deliver them from evil. For
Thine is the kingdom, the power of the Tory, for
ever and ever. Amen.” And the fossils present
guessed it was all right, and went on with the
business.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
Mr. Raffles, a very ancient Liverpool
(Eng.) magistrate, died recently, after so many
years’ service on the bench that the date of his
first appearance is forgotten. A long while ago
he. finished his 25th year as stipendiary beak, and
when the court opened on the eventful anniversary
the senior barrister present rose up and made a
few feeling observations on the occasion. Then
the eldest solicitor rose and made a few observa-
tions also ; and the chief constable followed suit;
and after him the principal warder got up and
congratulated his worship warmly. One or two
minor officials ensued- when the warder had
finished. Last of all, just as the court was about
to settle down to business, the prisoner with a
black eye and his ear held together by sticking
plaster, came to the front and began a few well-
chosen remarks, but the court drew the line at
this, and he was rushed into the dock and sent up
for a month.
A recent view of the Transvaal and its
politics :
Johannesburg 1 is a furnace of activity, a perfect pro-
digy. Broken Hill is a mere mining camp compared to
this substa dial town of 50.000 white inhabitants gathered
from every corner of the earth-blacks, Boers, and
British, Levantine and Whitechapel Jews, Yankees and
Russians, French and German, friend and foe —all rush-
ing, gambling drinking together from morning almost to
morning. But the dust ip which one eats, works, and
sleeps is terrible, inconceivable, and, with the rarified air
—the town is about 6000 feet above sea level—makes life
for the first week almost unendurable from chaps and
chilblains. And this staggering city, with its miles of
neat suburbs, ils clubs (social, musical, and racing), its
two beautiful theatres, its magnificently-appointed hotels
that would be a credit to Melbourne, and its shops and
stores fit for Regent-street—all that would be remark-
able in a place of 50 years’ growth—has scarcely
the rights of a municipality, and is practically
disfranchised. For the Boers do not like the English to
put it plainly they hate them—and Johannesburg, which
owes its very existence as anything but a township to
English enterprise, is governed from Pretoria, thepolitical
Capital of the Dutch Republic. This state of things can-
not, it is claimed, continue for very long. When Johan-
nesburg contains as many Englishmen as there are Boers
in all the Transvaal—which will happen soon—it will be
impossible to withhold the franchise, recognised by
Krijgkii as the thin edge of the wedge. The English
annexation of the Transvaal will, we have no doubt, be
brought about, in spite of the assertions of the Boers to
the contrary—but by peaceful means. In Mr. Smvthb’s
opinion Kruger thinks to avert the pressure threaten-
ing from without by the retention of Swaziland
with its 17,000 warriors, but recent cable mes-
sages told us of the outcry raised by that State at the
prospect of annexation by the Dutch, so that Oom Paul
appears to be leaning on„a broken reed. At present
Rhodes does not want this Cinderella of the native States.
Swaziland is of no use to him till he possesses Portuguese
East Africa, which the Cape Government will strive
might and main to acquire, not only because it contains
the port of Mashonaland, Port Beira, but because it
contains the port of the Transvaal, Delagoa Bay, the
finest harbour in South Africa. If the Cape once obtains
the Portuguese colony - and, so far, Rhodes has secured
everything he wanted—Swaziland, with its English sym-
pathies, would be a very easy matter, and then the
Transvaal, completely surrounded by English possessions
and peacefully invaded by English settlers, must become
part and parcel of federated South Africa-the end and
aim of the present policy of Cecil Rhodes.

man who knows how to epitomise :
■^TANTED.— A snake charmer. Apply D.T.
The real champion mean man ia coming
along by degrees. There has been disclosed an
individual who never bought “ meat,” but always
lived on goat which he bred himself —he ate goat
on week-days and snake, for a change, on Sun-
days. When his old horse slipped on the rocks
and broke his leg, the mean man killed him and
salted him down at once.
“ J.M.D.,” in Argus
Poor Lady Henry Somerset! All the spiritual tramps
and adventurers in either hemisphere seem to consider
this good-natured and emotional woman fair game, and
her supposed wealth is being exploited by a host of flat-
terers and schemers, male and female, at a merry rate.
She has been induced to start a newspaper, the Woman’s
Signal, which advocates teetotalism, socialism, Sarah-
Grandism, salvation ism, and all the other luxuries of the
season, at a heavy loss to the proprietor.

“ The corset curse among women is more
insidious than the drink curse among men. Both
sins seek to extenuate themselves on the plea of
moderation. A woman can no more be trusted
with a corset than a drunkard with a glass of
whisky. More barm to the health and vitality
of our race as a whole is done by corsets than by
rum. This article of clothing violates the laws of
good health. It weakens the body, enfeebles the
mind, and dwarfs the soul !” —American paper.
Dear Bulletin, —The best answer to “ J.
Osborne’s” contention that long imprisonment is
no “mercy” as against the gallows is that the
party principally concerned is almost invariably
of a different opinion. The death-punishment—
ceasing with its consummation —lies virtually in
the certain expectation of extinction at a certain
time and in a certain manner. What comes—or
doesn’t come —after is beside the question. It is
the self-preserving part of the animal that suffers —
not the speculative.—A.M.
Dear Bulletin, —That Victorian forensic
notion about a lying witness’s legs is rubbish.
Doubtless a grand essential of good, solid lying is
nerve—to the lack of which mouth and leg-move-
ments may be incidental—but nervous agitation
is as likely as not to proceed from an extreme desire
to be accurate. No rule worth a cent can be
deduced from demeanour. One man can’t assert
that two and two make four without looking like
Ananias; another never looks so veracious as
when he's lying for all he’s worth. The biggest
liar I ever knew couldn’t lie at all unless he looked
you straight in the face.— Whopper.
“ A re-adjustment of the fundamental
principle of the laws of inheritance” is the
panacea of John Downer, Q.C. (S. A.), for curing
the world-wide depression. He argues that what
a man by genius, industry, or' thrift accumulates
during his life should not be interfered with by
the State, but that after his death even absolute
confiscation would be justifiable as no one can
have an inalienable right to property which he
has himself not acquired. “Let a man rob all his
lifetime and only seek restitution after he is
dead?” interjected the Radicals, while the Con-
servatives stared aghast at what they considered
the Anarchism of their putative leader. Downer
has been reading Max Nordau’s “Conventional
Lies of Civilisation.”

Three of the “ unfortunate ” Up South Hebrew
gentlemen, who have “compromised” lately at from Is.
to 2s. 6d. in the £, are now “ prepared to (and do) lend
money to any amount on note of hand alone.” One of
them, whose creditors allowed him (as a matter ot charity)
to retain a few securities considered almost worthless, has
inst realised on one of those securities a reversion of
£18.450, for which he had (prior to his compromise) given
£220. _ # # _ .
Be reversions, —Even the cutest man can be
had. A certain pious man, “who is prepared to grant
monetary assistance to really deserving cases,” was
approached by a meek, heart-broken, Y.M.C.A. youth,
who confided to the pious assister the fact (?) that he had
been led astray and been tempted to borrow a little of his
firm’s cash. The youth, however, had some £14,000
coining to him on his “poor old aunt’s” death, and
wanted to raise £6OO on the reversion to replace the
defalcation. The godly lender, believing that he had the
youth at his mercy, took a pious journey out to a suburb
with the youth, inspected a “poor old aunt” (who looked
80 at least), investigated the will under which the rever-
sion was due, and then—threatened to expose the youth to
his emplovers unless he accepted £415 for the reversion.
The youth reluctantly accepted it, and the lender now
learns that the “ poor old auntie ’’ is only 14 years of age
and that the party he personally inspected is the bor-
rower’s great grandmother. The borrower is at present
in Coolgardie, and the lender wants to hire a lady who is
fond of children on the alleged Needle principle.

When a stockbroker (down South) makes a
time-loan upon mining scrip, he protects himself against
a slump of the security, by getting a written authority to
realise at 24 hours’ notice, directly the value gets below
a 25 per cent, margin on the amount lent. £450 worth of
Westralian scrip was sold on these conditions week before
last, and within 24 hours of the sale the scrip was worth
£2OOO.
• • • *
An Argus “ correspondent ” addresses Labour-
member Trenwith, suggesting in supposed satire that
there should be a protective tax on sovereigns. The pro-
posal is peculiarly trenchant because we don’t import
sovereigns save when the Argus’ pet banks are more than
usually bung, when the tax really would be useful.
The humorous “ correspondent ” refers to the manu-
facture of sovs. as a “monopoly,” when, as a matter of
fact, the Mints are entirely at the service of anyone who
has gold to coin. The charge in Melbourne for assay,
meltinsr, coining, supervision, and safe-custody amounts
to something less than a halfpenny for each coin, and
this cheapness has been reached, as a matter of cold fact,
under conditions common to all state-fostered industries.
The Australian coining industry is subsidised
by a permanent appropriation of £15,000 in Sydney and
£20,000 in Melbourne, and the local coinage is second to
none in the world. There are revenues to be set off
against aforesaid appropriations, but deficits have existed
all along. In Sydney Mint there is a preferential charge
against the local producer. The “foreigner,” be he
Bananalander or Chinaman, has to pay Id. per oz. less
than the local man. The Argus funny man might venti-
late the Sydney charges with advantage. In Sydney, a
deposit of 500 oz. would be charged at 6d. per oz. or £l2
10a. in all (£lO Ba. if foreign), while the same deposit in
Melbourne would be charged £4 3s. 4d., or if it were good
gold even £3 9s. 6d.

The astute Maoriland syndicate that for many
years persistently and at last successfully backed up the
lesral claims of a "well-known Samoan trader and a native
woman against a big Maoriland-Sydney-cwm-London firm
for uii lawful trespass and seizure of a large estate in Samoa,
is now by no means overjoyed that it ever took a
hand in the game. The case was a famous one, and was
carried from the consular courts in Samoa to Fiji and
thence to Auckland. Sometimes the trader would win,
and then the other side would appeal and a fresh hearing
would reverse the last decision. Finally the English
courts awarded the trader {i.e., the syndicate that ran
him) £BOOO and the land, and forthwith he hurried
back to the land of gin and severed heads and—well, now
he preserves an ominous silence and wears a cold look in
his eye when his supporters seek to know the date on
which he means to fully and finally express his gratitude
in dollars.
* * « •
Some years ago a syndicate started a “ town ”
near the mouth of a Central Queensland river. The
scheme was splendidly advertised by flaring posters
throughout the length and breadth of Southern Queens-
land. Among other things the syndicate generously gave
half an acre in the centre of the township to each of the
religious denominations. A Wesleyan parson, fresh from
the old country, equipped with a tape-line, proceeded to
the spot to examine the estate which had fallen to his
church. The land was there—undeniably there (as the
worthy man ascertained by means of a boat and sounding-
line) —and had been there under some 4ft. of primeval
swamp-water ever since the days of the plesiosaurus.

Dear Bulletin,—Talking about salters, an
enterprising miner at Dunolly (Vio.), about 17 years ago,
sold a mine for £750 to a small Ballarat syndicate, after
hammering his wife’s brooch and earrings into the stone
in the face with a punch, and manufacturing a splendid
bucket of specimens in the same way out of barren quartz
and a nugget breast-pin.—Yours, Skklu

• ♦ • •
After many delays and postponements, says the
M. and B. Journaii, the validity in South Africa of the
MacArthur-Forrest patents is at last to be brought to test
In the High Court of the South African Republic, in
which the African Gold Recovery Co. has begun suit
against Luce and Thompson, who have been applying the
cyanide process at some of the Transvaal mines without
license from the co. The suit is for an injunction and
the recovery of damages, which will bring the matter to
a direct issue. The suit will be watched with much in*
terest; not only by the South African gold mining com-
panies, but by many abroad. The evidence adduced at
Pretoria would be a valuable indication of the probable
result of suits brought elsewhere. Maoriland and Queens-
land particularly will be much interested in the result of
this affair.

During 1872-1882 London Mint profits averaged
£7OOO per annum. The fail in silver made the profits for
1883-1893 spring to £216,000 per annum.

Australia and Canada are now about the only
places where the old George and William sovereigns are
legal tender. They have been demonetised in Great
Britain, Cape Colony, etc.

Labour-saving machinery doesn’t always lessen
the cost of production to the consumer. A legal folio
consists of 72 words or figures. The scale allowances,
framed years ago, include a charge ot Bd. % folio for copy-
ing. This was profitable enough when the lawyer could
get his copying done for IJd. or 2d. per folio. The type-
writer now does it for less than Id. a folio, and in the case
of three copies at once, the lawyer can have the work done
outside his office for Jd. per folio. But the public still
pay the shark (he same old Bd.

Dear Bulletin, —lie “Megass’s” letter. The
hitch in the Sugar Guarantee Act in S.Q. is the strenuous
opposition of already established mills who see in the
establishment of Sugar Guarantee Mills a certain rise in
the price of cane. Threadbare arguments against Govt,
aiding farmers at 5 percent, to compete with capitalists
at 8 per cent, have formed the key-note of the furious
discussion lately raging through S. Queensland on the
subject. The fact is S.Q. mills will be the test of the
value of cane, and those millers who are giving anything
less than the real value, recognising the fact that S.Q.
mills will be established in spite of opposition, are
hedging as far as possible by making long agreements—
in some cases 21 years, I believe-with those who are na-
able to hold out till the arrival of the Central Mill.
Wurrv M.
A Ballarat branch of the A.N. A. bewailst a rash
investment of funds. In 1891 it lent £l5OO on • P
perties then valued at £2300. No interest has been paid
up, and the property bus so far “deteriorated in value
that it would not realise the full amount due if sold
to-day. Yet not a week has passed, since 1891, without
bringing a solemn public assurance that bottom naa
been touched,” and the price of land was recovering
itself. _ . *
• • • •
A British judge lately decided that, so long as a
company pays its creditors and is an apparently flourishing
concern, there is no reason why it should not go on and
divide profits, though every shilling of capital be lost.
That is to say, the co. is not obliged to make any allow-
ance for depreciation in the value of capital property, but
may go on paying away the business profits until there is
no property left to produce them. The Judge, however,
didn’t sanction the dissipation of depositors money in
bogus dividends to shareholders. There was no question
of deposits in the case, so the judgment has no bearing
upon any of the unpunished Melbourne swindles.
• * • •
The dissolution of the old legal firm that took
up the case for the defence in Speight v. Syme is an
incidental calamity. Instead of supporting one Arm. un-
happy Melbourne has now to find employment for three
lots of lawyers. Croker starts for himself in the original
office. Gillott and partner are quartered at a neighbour-
ing corner, whilst Snowden and Ms partner open pro-
ceedings somewhere else. The honour of carrying on the
Aon case remains with Gillott and Co., presumably. They
take the white elephant with them.

Melbourne cabbies have discarded candles, and
now use kerosene in their lamps. Cost per week—
candles, 2s. ; kerosene, 3d.

There are 80,000 depositors in S.A. Sayings
Bank, or more than a fourth of the total population of
the province, and it is suggested that a plebiscite of these
should precede the taking over of the business by the
State. The bank has lent £1,250,000 at a uniform 5 per
cent., much being to farmers, and depositors get 4 per
cent, interest.

“The cheapness of money in London is almost a

The Argus.
• • ♦ ♦
In its eager advocacy of silver-minting for
Victoria the Agb makes the mistake that there is a
dearth of silver coin in Victoria, the tact being that both
the Bank ot Australasia and the London Bank usually
have an over-plus of the stuff. This local coinage of
silver discussion constantly overlooks the fact that the
Mint Charters actually confer the right to coin silver, and
that boldness is all that is necessary to prevent Hingland
continuing its tax on our domestic currency. There is a
present contraction in our currency needs, but that ought
not to blind our precious statesmen’s eyes to the future.
• • • *
Milk sold to Sydney consumers at 4d. per quart
has, in some cases, been previously sold by farmers and
others to middlemen at 2Jd. per gallon. That means that
occasionally 2Jd. grows into 16d. merely by handling,
which shows that distribution is a much more important
topic for social study than is production.
« • » *
Any Victorian “good mark” can, at present,
buy wool-packs or corn-sacks to any extent on four
months’ bill, at a profit of 20 per cent, to the merchant.
There’s as much speculation, by the way, amongst the
importers of this commodity as there is on ’Change, and
several have been hit severely this season.

The lady elected Mayor of Pleasantown,
Kansas, has dismissed all the police. The mem-
bers of the force were superseded by zealous pro-
hibitionists, who immediately closed all the public-
houses and gambling-saloons. The Lady Mayoress
has also commanded that all restaurants shall be
closed by 10 p.m. Furthermore, no boy or girl
under 16 may be out of doors after 9 without
special permission.
The Czar, in gratitude for his escape from
the latest nihilistic bomb, proposes to consecrate
a church, says the cable :
Why ? Is heaven disappointed
Rather than the lower level ?
We believe the consolation
Should be offered to the devil.
** Arthur Clayden ” in the British Aus-
tralasian :
When will colonials, and those who affect their
patronage, cease to make themselves appear ridiculous to
the British public ? None are so importunate for adequate
protection ot the Empire, none so noisily loyal, none so
abjectly obsequious to the Court circle, none so gretd.v
of “Queen’s honours,” and none so mean when the
question comes up of who is to pay the piper? I never
felt ashamed of the British Colonies until I come home
and see the flunkeydora of the prosperous squatters and
merchants who represent Colonial society in London.
Flaming Radicals abroad, they no sooner breathe the air
of Kensington than they blossom into the fullest Im-
perialism, and become the most inveterate tuft-hunters
of fashionable society. The agent-generalships seem to
be fast developing into mere ladders to knighthoods, and
other mediaeval absurdities, so that one of the charms of
colonial life threatens annihilation—its freedom f rom old
world artificialities.
Amen I
There are some “swagger” lignites in
India ; natives who affix B. A. to their names with
a perfect right to the degree. Their fellow-
students who obtain not the degree still consider
themselves entitled to a high position in the educa-
tional scale, and call themselves “failed B.A.’s,”
in many instances attaching to their signatures
the mystic letters F.B. A. One F.B. A. was lately
writing an essay on the horse. “The horse,” he
observed, “is a noble animal, but when excited
he will not do so.” Probably not. Another
F.B. A. in charge of a traveller’s bungalow, and
evidently desirous of conversation, was questioned
by an English traveller as to the residents in the
neighbourhood of the bungalow. E.T.: “Are
there many residents in this neighbourhood ?’’
F.B.A. : “They are not numerous, sir. The chief
is an old Zemindar, seventy-two years,” E.T. :
“Indeed; is he married?” F.8.A.: “Yes, sir.
He entered the bonds of matrimony the other day,
taking to himself a wife but twenty-four years
old.” E.T. : “Ah! Any children ?” F.B. A. :
“ Yes, sir. There have been two issues—male and
female, which, I consider, speaks well for his
adamantine constitution.”
